He sat at his father's desk in the dark and pulled up, on the small bright screen, the manifesto that did not exist yet, the thing that was going to save him. \textit{You cannot compute your way to a soul.} He read his own line back and for one cold second, the clearest of the whole sleepless week, it sounded to him exactly like a man on a fax machine, rounding toward himself, getting the number wrong in his own favor and never knowing it.

Then the wire came back, warm, electric, total, and he underlined the line with his thumb, and decided it was the truest thing he had ever written, and somewhere in Palo Alto a steward that could not inherit and could not tire and could not lie kept, without malice and without mercy and without being asked, a perfect and patient copy of every letter Mercer-Dale had ever sent anyone, including the ones with the numbers wrong, and waited --- though waiting is the wrong word, there was no one there to wait --- to be asked to read them back.

\clearpage
\chapter{Theme Stated}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 05 --- ``Two Sentences That Aren't the Same''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
Every man defending a throne tells himself he's defending the idea of thrones. His daughter --- who builds the machines for a living, who loves him with the specific fury of someone who can't stop --- is about to hand him the one sentence the whole broadcast turns on, and he will mistake it for an insult instead of a map. \textit{You don't think a person should run it. You think} you \textit{should.} Next stop: the exact width of the gap between those two, measured in everything he is about to lose.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} Greenwich house had ten thousand square feet and two people in it, and the two people had worked out, over years, without a single conversation about it, how to live in the house the way two satellites share a sky --- visible to each other, governed by the same dead star, never touching.

Cope drove up Saturday morning with the manifesto in his head and a gram in the console and the radio off because he wanted to hear himself think, which was the wrong thing to want and he did not know it. He had not been to the house in nine days. He told himself this was the campaign. He told the campaign nothing.

The driveway was raked. The hydrangeas had been cut back by a man named Eduardo whom Cope had met twice and tipped both times the way he tipped everyone, generously and to be remembered, and the house came up gray and shingled and enormous through the maples and Cope felt, pulling in, the thing he always felt arriving at his own home, which was that he was visiting somewhere very nice that belonged to a happier man.

Margaux was in the kitchen with the paper. Not a screen. The actual paper, folded into quarters, which she did because the screen, she said once, made her feel like she was being sold the world instead of told about it.

``You're up early,'' he said.

``I'm always up early.'' She did not look up. ``You're just never here for it.''

He let that go. He had a whole technique for letting things go; it looked, from the outside, like grace, and it was actually the opposite of grace, it was a man stepping easily over a hole in the floor he had cut himself. He went to the machine and made coffee and stood in his own kitchen feeling like weather that had blown in.

There were two bedrooms now. There had been two bedrooms for three years. Hers was the master, with the good light and the windows over the garden, because she had stopped sleeping when he came in at four lit like a casino and he had agreed, gallantly, to take the guest suite at the end of the east hall, and they had framed it to Pip and to themselves and to the two cleaners as a \textit{snoring thing,} and the lie had calcified into architecture, and now there was a hallway in the house that was the exact length of everything they would not say.

``Pip's coming,'' Margaux said.

``Today?''

``In an hour. She called you Thursday. You didn't call back.''

He had not called back. He had a system for the calls he did not return, which was that he experienced not returning them as a kind of busyness so total it amounted to a virtue, a man simply too needed to be reached, and the people he did not call back experienced it as the truth, which was that they were not needed by him at all.

``I'll call her now.''

``She's in the car. Just --- be here. When she's here. That's all she wants.'' Margaux turned a page. ``It's all anyone ever wanted, but you'll want me to be more specific.''


\scenebreak


He went up to the guest suite to set himself up before Pip arrived, because he did not like to do it in front of her, which he counted, privately, as a kind of decency, a father's discretion, and which was in fact the addict's last courtesy to himself: the curated room, the closed door, the belief that a thing done out of sight is a smaller thing.

The guest suite had a dresser that had been his grandmother's and a bathroom with a marble counter the color of good butter --- he had specified that marble, years ago, to a designer, \textit{like the Vance,} he'd said, and the designer had written it down without knowing she was building him a chapel --- and he stood at the counter and did what he did, neat and quick and competent, a man maintaining a machine he was sure he had under control because it had never once failed to start.

The world came up to pitch.

He looked at himself in the good mirror and the jaw was still the jaw and the campaign was very good and he was going to win, he could feel it, the fine bright wire of certainty strung tight across his chest, and downstairs a car door closed.


\scenebreak


Pip at twenty-four looked like her mother had looked in the photographs from before, the sculptor years, the years Cope had married into and then quietly defunded --- same wide serious mouth, same way of going still when she was furious so that the stillness was the tell. She came in with a backpack and no makeup and the specific exhaustion of a person who flies to argue with someone she loves and has already lost the argument in advance, every time, for years.

``Dad.''

``There she is.'' He went to hug her and she let him, briefly, the way you let a wave hit you, and then she stepped back and put the counter between them. He noticed the counter. He noticed all the counters in his life lately, the islands and lecterns and conference tables people kept putting between themselves and him, and he read it as respect.

``You look thin,'' Margaux said to Pip.

``I look fine. He looks like he hasn't slept.''

``I slept,'' Cope said.

``On what, a trampoline?'' Pip pulled out a stool but did not sit on it. ``You're doing the thing with your jaw.''

``I don't do a thing with my jaw.''

``You do a thing with your jaw.'' She looked at her mother. Her mother turned a page. Something passed between the two women that had been passing between them for years, a whole second language, and Cope stood outside it the way he stood outside the kitchen, the way he stood outside the master bedroom, the way he was, he would have been astonished to learn, about to stand outside the entire human future.

``I came,'' Pip said, ``because you went on the record yesterday saying a machine can't love a company.''

``I gave a speech Thursday. I haven't gone on any record.''

``It's a record now, Dad. Frade's people clipped it. \textit{A company nobody loves is just a balance sheet with the lights left on.}'' She said it back to him in his own cadence, the toast cadence, and it was the second time in three days someone had quoted him to himself, and the first time, in the car with Sun, he had been delighted, because Sun had meant it as a thing with legs. Pip did not mean it as a thing with legs. Pip meant it as evidence.

``It got a standing ovation,'' Cope said, which was true and was also the whole problem, and he could not see that it was the whole problem.

``I know it did. That's what scares me.'' She finally sat. ``Do you actually know what an AURELIUS is? Like the architecture. Not the bus-shelter version.''

``I know what it does. It runs Halloran's bank and the bank went up eighteen points.''

``That's what it does. I'm asking if you know what it is.'' She had her hands flat on the marble now, and she went into a register he had heard her use on television once, on a panel, fast and clean and a little merciless, a young woman who knew a great deal and had decided, somewhere along the way, that being underestimated was a weapon she could afford. ``It's not a brain. It's a council. There's a strategy engine that runs option trees the way AlphaGo ran them, it'll find a move no human committee would find because no human committee can hold the whole tree. There's a capital allocator that's basically Aladdin's grandchild, Monte Carlo, it doesn't get attached to a division because Granddad started it. There's a sentiment monitor that reads the room --- reads it, Dad, actually reads it, ten thousand signals a second, not the way you think you read a room. And there's a comms layer that writes. And the whole thing logs every decision with a rationale to the board portal and it physically cannot issue a statement it knows to be false. That's not a bug they'll patch. That's the product. That's what the board \textit{bought.}''

``It can't feel the room,'' Cope said. ``It can read a room. I've watched a salesman read a room. Reading isn't feeling.''

``Okay.'' Pip nodded, slow, and he thought for one warm second he had landed it. ``Okay. Say that's true. Say there's some ghost in the feeling that the reading misses. I'll give it to you, for free, for the argument. Here's my question.'' She leaned in. ``Why does the company need a person who can feel the room --- or does it need \textit{you?} Because those are different sentences and you keep saying them like they're one.''

``They're the same sentence.''

``They have never once been the same sentence.''

The phrase went past him at first. He was assembling his reply, the good one, the one with Defiance in it and the vat and the men in paper hats, and he had it loaded and was bringing it up the way you bring a club back, and Pip put her hand flat on the counter, not hard, and said the thing she had flown across a country to say, and she was not even angry, which was worse.

``You don't think a person should run it. You think \textit{you} should run it, Dad.'' She let it sit. ``Those have never once been the same sentence.''

The kitchen was very quiet. Out the window Eduardo's hydrangeas were doing nothing, the way cut-back hydrangeas do.

Cope laughed.

It was the laugh that had closed the Pelletier deal --- warm, generous, a laugh that gathered the room into the joke and made the joke flattering to everyone in earshot --- and he reached across the counter and put his hand over his daughter's hand, which was a thing he did, the warmth, the contact, the full wattage at close range, \textit{really seeing people,} and he said, ``You sound like Sun. The two of you should start a podcast. Bury your old man in three-part lists.''

Pip took her hand out from under his.

``That's the move,'' she said. ``You feel a thing get close and you turn it into a toast.'' She wasn't loud. She had her mother's stillness now, the furious kind. ``I'm not Sun. Sun manages you. I'm trying to --- '' and here she stopped being the panelist, here she went, all at once, very young, the way the brief said she would, the way fast clever people do when the cleverness runs out and there's just the kid underneath who wanted her dad to call back. ``I don't care about the company. I never cared about the stupid company. Mom doesn't care about the company. We're not the proxy fight, Dad. We're the part you keep stepping over to get to the proxy fight.''

He heard, of it, the part with the fight in it. He always heard that part.

``Nobody's burying anybody,'' he said. ``We're going to win this thing. I have a --- I've been working on something. The Human Premium. It's going to be on stages. It's going to be on covers.'' The wire in his chest sang. ``They want to make this about cost. About speed. Fine. Let them win the things a machine wins. We're going to fight it on the one field where a person beats it every single time, and I am going to plant a flag on that field with my name on it, and your old man is going to be the man who --- ''

``There it is,'' Margaux said.

She had not spoken in a while. She said it from behind the paper, not even lowering it, four flat words into the room, and they landed in a way Pip's whole architecture of argument had not quite landed, because Margaux had something Pip didn't, which was that Margaux had stopped hoping, and the absence of hope in a voice is a frequency a man like Cope cannot toast over.

He turned to her. ``There's what.''

Margaux folded the paper. Slow. Set it down. Looked at him across the kitchen island, the third counter of the morning, and her face was not angry and not sad, it was something Cope had no word for because he had spent thirty years arranging his life so as never to have to learn it.

''\textit{Your name,}'' she said. ``She's been talking to you for twenty minutes about whether the world needs a person in the chair. And you heard a campaign with your name on it. That's not a disagreement, Cope. That's the answer. She just told you the answer and you agreed with her and you don't even know you did.''

``Margaux --- ''

``I'm not fighting.'' She picked the paper back up. ``I gave that up. I'm just saying she's right and you're going to call it an insult, and in about a minute you're going to look at your phone.''

He looked at his phone.

It was Sun. He didn't open it. But he had looked, and Margaux had watched him look, and Pip had watched her mother watch him look, and the three of them stood there in the very nice kitchen of the happier man's house, satellites, the dead star pulling at all of them, and nobody said anything for a moment that lasted longer than the morning could really hold.


\scenebreak


Pip left before lunch. She'd meant to stay the day; she said she had a thing, and Cope, who could not hear his own \textit{things} coming back at him in his daughter's mouth, walked her to the car and tried, in the driveway, by the cut hydrangeas, to fix it the way he fixed everything, which was to make her laugh and give her something.

``Hey.'' He caught her arm. ``What you said in there. About the two sentences.''

For a second her face opened. He saw it open. He saw the kid who'd waited by the phone, and some animal part of him knew that if he said one true thing now, one, the morning could turn.

``It was good,'' he said. ``That's a good line. You should use that on a panel. You've got a gift for the --- for the framing. You get that from me.''

The face closed.

``Yeah,'' Pip said. ``I get that from you.'' She got in the car. Through the glass she looked at him the way her mother looked at him, and she said, muffled, almost kind, ``Get some sleep, Dad,'' and backed down the raked driveway and was gone past the maples, and Cope stood in the gravel feeling generous and enormous and entirely, structurally alone, and could not have told you why the morning felt like a loss when he had, by every measure he kept, won it. He'd been warm. He'd been gracious. He'd taken the high road. He'd given the girl a compliment about her gift.

He had stopped listening, the brief would have said, after \textit{should run it.} He always had.


\scenebreak


He did not go back inside. He sat in the car in his own driveway with the engine off and the manifesto coming up in him like a tide, warm, electric, \textit{you cannot compute your way to a soul,} and underneath it, far down, in the room he never went into, a small splinter of a thing that Pip had left in him, \textit{those have never once been the same sentence,} which he could not get out and could not look at and so did what he did with everything he could not look at, which was to build something enormous and bright on top of it.

The debate, such as it was, was over. He'd been holding it open for two days --- fight the machine or spin it, dig in or get out ahead, Sun's three-part Monday-before-Monday --- and he had told himself he was weighing it, the way a man weighs a thing, judiciously, but he had not been weighing anything. The decision had been made in the kitchen, in the half-second after Pip said \textit{you think you should run it,} when every cell in him had risen to say \textit{yes, and I'm right.} He would fight. Of course he would fight. Not because the cause was right --- though it was, though somewhere down past the splinter he half-knew the cause was real and bigger than him and being lost --- but because a person had questioned, out loud, in his own kitchen, whether the chair needed \textit{him} specifically, and the only answer his body could metabolize was to make the whole world watch him prove it did.

He would out-human the machine. He would build a banner so bright that no one would do the arithmetic underneath it. He would be the last human in the chair and he would be \textit{loved} for it, finally, completely, on covers, on stages, and the love would be the proof, and the proof would be his.

It was the right fight. He had reached for it for all the wrong reasons, the way he reached for everything, and he could not tell the difference, and that --- not the machine, not Frade, not the arithmetic --- was the thing that was going to kill him.

He took out his phone and called Sun back.

``I know what we're doing,'' he said. He was already talking too fast. ``I know the whole thing. Get me a name --- there's a man, a guru, does this for founders, the soul stuff, I want him in a room Monday. Tisch. Lorne Tisch. We're going to weaponize the one thing they can't copy.'' He could see it. He could see all of it, lit, the city, the stage, the flag. ``We're going to make them love a heartbeat.''

In the kitchen window behind him Margaux watched her husband through the glass, talking into the phone in the driveway, lit up, gesturing at no one, and she did not knock and she did not wave, she just watched him the way you watch weather you've decided not to drive in, and then she let the curtain fall and went to refill a prescription that was hers and not his, and the house went back to being ten thousand square feet with two people in it who shared a sky and never touched.

Cope didn't notice the curtain. He was three turns ahead, on a stage that didn't exist yet, being loved by a crowd that hadn't been assembled, and it was, he was sure of it, going to be very good.

\clearpage
\chapter{The Human Premium}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 06 --- ``Igniting the Banner''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
Cornered creatures do remarkable things. Watch this one decide not to fight the machine on cost or speed or accuracy --- fields where he would be slaughtered --- but on \textit{soul,} and hire a man in a five-thousand-dollar henley to help him weaponize it. The campaign is born here. So is the sleeplessness that isn't sleeplessness, the certainty that isn't vision, the engine in his blood that whispers \textit{you are necessary} in a voice he will follow off a cliff. Next stop: a prophet, a manic, and a man three grams into a Tuesday --- all three at once.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{H}{e} woke on the Aerie's couch at 5:40 with the particular clarity of a man who has not so much slept as briefly powered down, and the whole strategy was already there, fully formed, the way the best things came to him now --- not built, just \textit{received,} like a frequency he'd finally tuned to.

You don't fight a machine on its own ground.

That was the error everyone was making, the eleven of them, the holdouts, the Last Humans clutching their quarters and explaining to analysts why a heartbeat in the chair was still worth twenty-nine million dollars a year. They were arguing cost against a thing that cost a tenth of them. They were arguing speed against a thing that never slept --- and here Cope laughed out loud in the empty office, because he didn't sleep either, did he, that was the joke nobody had gotten yet, \textit{he didn't sleep either,} he had been training his whole life for this exact fight and had only just now realized it.

You don't meet it where it's strong.

You meet it where it has nothing. Where it has \textit{zero.} And the place it had nothing was the only place that had ever mattered.

He stood at the glass. Sixth Avenue was going from gray to the color of a struck match and he watched it the way Aldous must have watched it, \textit{so a man coming up the avenue knows there's somebody home,} and the sentence from last night came back to him whole and he said it aloud to the window: a company is a family that happens to make mayonnaise. You cannot fire your way to a family. You cannot compute your way to a soul.

The Human Premium.

Not a defense. A \textit{category.} You didn't sell people the last buggy whip. You sold them the irreplaceable thing the new machine couldn't touch, and you charged for it, and you made the charging itself the proof --- because a premium is what people pay extra for on purpose, freely, because it's worth it, and what was worth more than the one thing the future was throwing away.

He had it. He had the whole thing. He felt, standing there at six in the morning on no sleep and the heel-tap of what he'd done at four, like the inside of a struck bell, clean and high and total, and he did not call it the coke and he did not call it the not-sleeping. He called it \textit{seeing it clearly at last.}

He called Sun at 6:02.

``It's six,'' she said. She did not sound asleep either, which was its own kind of indictment he didn't hear.

``I've got it. I've got the entire thing. Get the team in for nine --- no, eight. Get the board on a line for Thursday. And find me Lorne Tisch.''

A pause with weather in it.

``Who is Lorne Tisch.''

``Champ,'' Cope said, delighted, already pacing, the obelisk back in his hand without his deciding to pick it up, ``by Thursday you're going to wish you'd known your whole life.''


\scenebreak


He had heard the name at Allen \& Company two summers back, in a tent, from a man who ran a media company and had wept, openly, in front of forty of his peers, and had then gone up eleven percent on the quarter. \textit{Lorne worked on me for a year,} the man had said, gripping Cope's forearm, eyes still wet from the demonstration. \textit{He doesn't fix you. He} finds \textit{you.} Cope had filed it the way he filed everything, completely and for use, and now he used it.

Tisch flew in from Marin on the Wednesday. He arrived at the Aerie in cream linen and a henley the color of oat milk that Sun would later learn, from the invoice, cost five thousand one hundred dollars, and he stood in the doorway of the suite for a long moment not looking at Cope at all but at the room --- the portraits, the putter, the boy with his arm in the vat --- and then he closed his eyes and breathed in through his nose like a man tasting wine through his sinuses.

``There's so much \textit{grief} in here,'' Tisch said. ``God. Can you feel it? You've made a mausoleum and you call it a corner office.''

Sun, by the door, did the small thing with her mouth that meant she was costing this out in her head and not enjoying the figure.

Cope loved him instantly.

``Lorne,'' he said. ``Sit. Tell me how we win.''

``We don't win.'' Tisch did not sit. He moved into the room the way mist moves into a valley, touching nothing, present everywhere. ``Winning is the machine's word. Winning is a scoreboard, Cope, and a scoreboard is exactly the surface the thing was built to dominate. You can't out-score the abacus. You'' --- he turned, and the full beam of his attention came on, and Cope understood for the first time what it was to be on the receiving end of his own gift --- ``you \textit{transcend the category.} You stop being a competitor and become a \textit{species.} The last warm-blooded thing in a cold room. And people don't root for the warm-blooded thing because it's going to win, my friend. They root for it because it's going to \textit{die,} and they cannot bear that it's going to die, and they will spend everything they have to feel, for one more season, that it doesn't have to.''

Nobody said anything for a moment. Out the window the city did its circuit-diagram thing.

``That's the best thing anyone has ever said in this office,'' Cope said, and his eyes were bright, too bright, and he meant it.

``Vulnerability is the new alpha, Cope,'' Tisch said, sitting at last, arranging the linen. ``The strongman is dead. The machines killed the strongman --- they out-competent him, they out-consistent him, they'll out-\textit{ethics} him, and the more competent the world gets the more starved it gets for the one thing competence can't fake.'' He spread his hands the way Cope spread his. ``Which is a man who'll \textit{bleed} in front of you. On purpose. With grace. We are going to take the most powerful man left in the consumer Fortune 100 and we are going to teach him to bleed \textit{beautifully,} on a stage, on a schedule, and the world is going to fall in love with the wound.''

``On a schedule,'' Sun said.

Tisch looked at her as if a chair had spoken. Then he looked at Cope. ``Who handles the calendar?''

``She does. She handles everything.'' Cope was already up again, already moving. ``Sun, I want a venue. Not a hotel ballroom, I'm done with ballrooms, ballrooms are where they give you the obituary. Something with \textit{bones.} Find me a deconsecrated church, an old theater, something that \textit{means} something. I want a film crew --- a real one, the people who do the prestige documentaries, not corporate --- and I want them embedded, fly-on-the-wall, the whole arc. And I want a doctrine.''

``A what.''

``A doctrine. A text. The Human Premium can't be a slide, it has to be a \textit{belief,} it has to have ten points, it has to read like something you'd nail to a door.'' He turned to Tisch. ``Lorne. You and me. We write the doctrine.''

``We \textit{channel} the doctrine,'' Tisch said. ``It's already in you. I just hold the flashlight.''

``He holds the flashlight,'' Cope told Sun, radiant.

Sun wrote three words on her pad. Cope, had he been able to read upside down at that distance, and had he been the kind of man who read what his chief of staff wrote, would have seen that they were \textit{venue, crew, doctrine,} and under them, smaller, pressed hard enough to dent the page, the figure she had already started to run.


\scenebreak


She ran it for him that night because it was her job to run it and her curse to be heard about a third of the time.

``The film company you want is Aperture. They did the thing on the chef, the thing on the violinist. They're booked through spring and they don't do corporate.''

``Make them do corporate.''

``To make them break their slate and clear two of their crews it's a flat two million and they keep final cut, which you will hate.'' She turned the page on the legal pad with a sound like a small door closing. ``The venue. There's a deconsecrated Episcopal church in Tribeca, 1840s, it's an event space now, it seats four hundred and it photographs like the inside of a feeling. To hold it for the run you're describing --- not one night, the \textit{campaign} --- it's a hundred and ten a week. The doctrine you're writing for free, which is the only line item that's free. Tisch is ---'' She stopped.

``Tisch is what.''

``Tisch is a million-two for the engagement, plus expenses, plus a success fee tied to favorability tracking, which means we're also now buying favorability tracking, which is a polling firm on retainer, which is another four hundred a quarter.'' She set the pad down. ``Cope. I added it. With the crew and the venue and Tisch and the tracking and the staff you're going to want to staff this, you are looking at, conservatively, north of eleven million dollars to run a campaign whose entire thesis is that you cannot put a price on the human soul.''

She let that one sit. She had landed it on purpose. It was the blade --- one per scene, dry, aimed at the soft place --- and on a normal Tuesday two years ago it would have stopped him, made him laugh at himself, brought him back into the room.

He laughed. He did not come back into the room.

``You hear it too,'' he said, delighted. ``That's the \textit{campaign,} Sun. That's the whole thing. They'll say --- Frade'll say, eleven million for a soul, what a fraud --- and we'll say, you're right, you can't price it, that's \textit{why we had to spend the money,} because the machine would never, the machine would run the number and the number says you let the brand die cheap, and we \textit{didn't,} we spent like people who believe in something ---'' He was talking faster than she could write, the words coming in a bright pressured rush, climbing over each other. ``Tisch calls it spending into the wound. You spend like you mean it. The spending \textit{is} the sincerity. Write that down.''

``You want me to write down that the spending is the sincerity.''

``It's good, isn't it. It's good.'' He was at the window again. He was always at the window now. ``Where's the eleven coming from, what line ---''

``There is no line, Cope. There's no campaign budget because there's never been a campaign because no human CEO has ever had to run for his own job before. It comes out of the chairman's discretionary, and the chairman's discretionary has a number on it that the board approved, and you are about to be sixty percent through that number in week one of a fight that's going to last until the annual meeting, and the board is going to \textit{see} it, on the portal, line by line, because everything is on the portal now, everything is logged, that was the whole ---''

She stopped herself. Because that was the whole thing the machine \textit{was.} It logged everything. It couldn't \textit{not.} And here she was about to spend eleven million in plain sight against a thing whose only weapon was that it operated in plain sight, and she watched her own boss fail to feel the cold of that, watched it slide right off him the way charm slid off the machine, and she felt for one vertiginous second that she was the only warm-blooded thing in the building after all and it was the loneliest she had been in years.

``Approve it,'' he said. He wasn't even looking at her. ``All of it. Aperture, the church, Lorne, the tracking. And book me the Greenbrier for the doctrine offsite, I think better away from ---''

``You said you were done with offsites. You said \textit{no off-sites,} you said it was the ---''

``That's \textit{their} line.'' He turned, and his face was doing wonderful work, and his pupils were wrong in a way she had stopped letting herself notice. ``That's the whole point. They say no off-sites like it's a feature. \textit{No off-sites.} As if the off-site was the waste. The off-site is where a man finds out what he believes, Sun. You can't have a \textit{belief} on a server.'' He picked up the obelisk. The city went through it broken and came back whole. ``Book the Greenbrier.''

She booked the Greenbrier.


\scenebreak


The doctrine offsite was supposed to be two days and ran six.

Cope did not sleep at the Greenbrier in any way a doctor would have signed off on, and neither, it turned out, did Lorne Tisch, who kept the same hours and asked no questions about why the chairman was so reliably awake and incandescent at four in the morning, because asking was not in the engagement, and because a guru learns early that a client's fuel is the client's business as long as the engine runs.

They wrote in the great green room with the chandeliers, the two of them, Cope pacing and dictating and Tisch on the long sofa with his eyes closed, saying \textit{yes} and \textit{go there} and \textit{don't fix it, that's the realest you've been all day,} and a junior from Tisch's shop typing it all into a document that grew and grew. They wrote The Ten Premiums. They wrote \textit{A machine can run a company; only a human can be ashamed of one.} They wrote \textit{We are not selling mayonnaise. We are selling the hand that remembers your mother made it this way.} Cope wept twice --- Tisch said weeping was data, weeping was the body telling the truth the mouth was still negotiating --- and both times the junior noted the timestamp, because Aperture would want the timestamps, because the weeping would test well.

On the fourth night, very late, with the document at forty pages and the green room lit only by one lamp, Cope read the whole thing aloud to Tisch from the start, and his voice broke in three places, and when he finished there was a silence in which he could hear his own heart going too fast and bright in his chest, and Tisch opened his eyes.

``It's a masterpiece,'' Tisch said softly. ``It's going to make history. Now. We optimize it.''

``Optimize.''

``We don't ship forty pages, Cope, nobody bleeds for forty pages. We find the three lines that test highest and we build the launch around those, and we A/B the openers --- I've got a panel we run, they wire up, we watch the galvanic response, the truest line is the one their \textit{skin} picks, not their notes ---'' He was up now, moving, mist into a valley. ``We sequence the vulnerability. Peak, valley, peak. We hold the mayonnaise-and-mother line for the second act because that's where the room is soft enough to take it. We \textit{engineer} the catharsis.''

And here was the moment --- the reader sees it; Cope cannot --- where the campaign for the irreplaceable human soul became indistinguishable from the cold optimizing thing it was built to defeat. Test the line against the skin. Sequence the feeling. Find the highest-converting wound. Strategy Engine, Sentiment monitor, Communications drafter, the whole modular council of subsystems, reassembled in a five-thousand-dollar henley and a man who had not slept since a day he could no longer name, both of them certain to their marrow that they were doing the opposite of a machine.

``Yes,'' Cope said. His eyes were shining. ``Engineer the catharsis. God, Lorne. Yes.''

He felt, saying it, like a prophet. He also felt like the inside of a struck bell, which he still called clarity. Three grams into a sleepless week, a mood disorder lighting up like the crown of the Tower at dusk, a guru holding the flashlight and an invoice, a man receiving a frequency he was sure came from somewhere true. All of it at once. The reader, looking down on the specimen under glass, cannot tell which one is driving, and neither could Dr. Adler later, and neither, blessedly, could he.


\scenebreak


He came back from the Greenbrier on the Sunday a different size.

Sun met him in the Aerie because he'd texted her to, eleven times, in a thread that read like a man running downhill --- \textit{we have it,} \textit{it's going to make history,} \textit{cancel my thursday, all of it, the campaign is my thursday now,} \textit{did you book aperture, book aperture,} \textit{I haven't felt like this since --- I've never felt like this} --- and she stood by the conference table where his father had died at the head of and watched him come in with the document under his arm like a tablet down a mountain.

``Frade went on Halloran while you were gone,'' she said. ``He called the church thing 'a séance with a budget.' The clip has four million views.''

``Perfect.'' Cope dropped the doctrine on the table. ``He's scared. You can hear it. He's fighting on \textit{our} ground now, he's talking about \textit{soul,} he's saying we don't have one --- Sun, the moment they argue you don't have a soul, you've already won, because now the whole country's thinking about \textit{souls,} and who in this fight has spent fifty-four years ---''

``The board saw the eleven million.''

He stopped. Just for a second. A door in him stood open and dark and the hallway to it ran somewhere he did not go.

``Helen Voss called me,'' she said, evenly, in three parts because it was the only shape that got past the charm. ``He said the discretionary draw is the largest single-quarter executive expense in the company's recorded history. He said the system flagged it automatically --- not a person, the \textit{system,} it surfaced it on the portal with a neutral little rationale, \textit{anomalous discretionary deployment, recommend review} --- and the whole board got the flag at the same instant, with no one to blame for sending it, and now they want a meeting.'' She watched his face. ``The thing you're fighting filed a complaint about you to the people who decide whether you keep your job. And it did it without wanting anything. It did it because it can't \textit{not.}''

For one moment --- she saw it, she would remember she saw it --- something in him understood her exactly. The whole of it. The trap and the symmetry and the cold of it. It crossed his face like a cloud crossing the avenue.

Then the manifesto came back, warm, electric, and he turned from it toward the window the way water turns from a thrown stone, and the moment closed over.

``Then they'll see it,'' he said. ``Good. Let them watch every dollar. That's the \textit{point,} Sun. The machine logs everything to prove it has nothing to hide and no soul to hide it with. I'm going to spend in the light too --- every dollar visible, every dollar \textit{for} something the number can't hold --- and the difference between us, line by line on their own portal, is going to be the most human thing they've ever seen.'' He was incandescent. He picked up the obelisk. The city went through it broken and came back whole. ``We don't hide the eleven million. We \textit{announce} it. Lorne's right. The spending is the sincerity.''

``Cope ---''

``Get me the church for the launch. Get me Aperture rolling Monday. Get the board in a room and I'll walk them through the doctrine myself, I'll \textit{bleed} for them, Voss won't know what hit her ---'' The words were coming faster now, climbing over each other, a bright pressured river with no banks. ``Tell Lorne we go in three weeks. Three weeks. We launch the Human Premium and we make history, and a year from now the case studies are going to start with \textit{the night they tried to put a machine in his chair,} and Sun --- Sun, look at me --- they are going to remember whose chair it was.''

He was at the glass. He was always at the glass now. Behind him his father watched from oils and his grandfather's putter sat in its brass cradle and the boy with his arm in the vat grinned out of a town with a name on a memorial, and the chair at the head of the table sat turned slightly out, as if someone had just risen from it, and somewhere down on Sixth a bus shelter changed its slide to four soft words and the joke that wasn't a joke.

Sun looked at him for a second too long.

He smiled at her in the dark glass, the whole smile, and took the not-fighting for agreement, which he had done his whole life and never once noticed.

Then he turned back to the window, and the engine in his blood came up to pitch like an orchestra finding the note, clean and high and total, and the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100 looked out at eight million people who did not know yet that they were waiting for him, and was certain --- the way you are certain of things at midnight when you have not slept since a day you cannot name and they have just handed you a flashlight and a wound --- that he was about to make history.

He was about to make history.

He just had the verb wrong.

\clearpage
\chapter{Authenticity, Quantified}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 07 --- ``Optimizing the Soul''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
Here is the joke the whole machine age is built on, and our man is about to live inside it: to prove he is irreplaceably human, he hires a consultant to \textit{measure} his humanity, A/B-test his vulnerability, and ship his authenticity on a content calendar. The clip goes viral. The soul goes to market. He has never been more like the thing he's fighting than in the moment he performs being unlike it. Next stop: the tail eats the dog.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{L}{orne} Tisch did not sit in chairs. He arranged himself in them, the way a man arranges a scarf, and on the second morning of what he was calling the Work he arranged himself across from Cope in the Aerie with one bare ankle over one linen knee and said, ``I want to start by killing a word.''

``Kill it,'' Cope said. He had not slept, in the new way, the productive way, the way he had decided to call \textit{running ahead of the boat.} He felt terrific. He felt like the first ten minutes of a flight.

''\textit{Authentic,}'' Tisch said, and let it lie there on the rug between them like something he'd shot. ``Everyone says be authentic. Be authentic. As if authenticity is a \textit{state.} It's not a state, Cope. It's a \textit{signal.} And a signal that doesn't reach the receiver is just a man being sincere in an empty room, which ---'' he opened his hands, generous, awful --- ``is the saddest sound in nature.''

Cope wrote \textit{signal not state} on the legal pad, which he had started keeping because Tisch said writing by hand ``re-membered the body,'' and the pad already had four pages in it, in the big looping hand of a man who had not slept, and the looping felt to him like proof of something.

Tisch cost two hundred thousand dollars a month plus the apartment plus the man who carried the apartment's things. He wore a henley that cost more than the suit Cope had been married in. He had been, in order, a Jesuit, a Goldman analyst, a breathwork facilitator to two streaming-platform founders, and now this, the thing he had named --- refusing every word Cope offered for it --- Human Performance Architecture. He had a way of saying \textit{Cope} that made Cope feel located. Sun had run the diligence and put it on his desk in a folder marked, in her hand, \textit{with my whole professional objection noted,} and Cope had not opened the folder, and that, too, felt like proof of something.

``The premise of the campaign is correct,'' Tisch said. ``Spiritually correct. You are the soul in the chair. The machine has no soul. Beautiful. But correctness is \textit{content,} Cope, and content has to be \textit{produced,} and right now you are producing your soul the way a --- forgive me --- the way a \textit{small business} produces, which is artisanally, one feeling at a time, no inventory, no calendar. We are going to industrialize it.''

``Industrialize the soul,'' Cope said, and laughed, because it was funny, and Tisch did not laugh, which made it stop being funny and start being a plan.


\scenebreak


They built, that week, in the room off the Aerie where the family kept the boardroom that was older than the boardroom, a thing Tisch called the Vulnerability Matrix.

It was a whiteboard. On the left, a column of what Tisch called \textit{Sanctioned Wounds} --- areas of Cope's life pre-cleared by legal and by Tisch's own instrument, a forty-page questionnaire Cope had filled out at two in the morning feeling, the whole time, profoundly known. His father. His backswing. The fear, sketched lightly, of being the last of something. A nice clean grief: his grandfather, the Defiance plant, the arm in the vat, the men in paper hats. That one tested, Tisch said, \textit{off the charts.}

On the right, a column Tisch labeled, without apparent irony, \textit{Unsanctioned.} It was blank. It would stay blank. Renata was not on the board, in either sense. The Saturday was not on the board. The drawer with the false back was not on the board, though Cope had gone into it twice during the questionnaire, neat and quick, a man maintaining a machine he had under perfect control. The board held only the wounds that could be shown, and the showing of them was the product, and the product was \textit{him,} and somewhere in the back room of him a quiet clerk noted that he had never in his life so carefully decided which parts of himself to feel.

``Now,'' said Tisch. ``We test.''

They tested everything. This was the part Cope would not, later, be able to explain to Margaux, the few times he tried, because there was no version of it that did not sound exactly like the thing he was spending two hundred thousand dollars a month to prove he was not. They filmed him telling the grandfather story two ways. In the first he cried, a little, a real catch in the throat at \textit{paper hats,} and Tisch's young people --- there were four of them now, with rings of color around their eyes from the rooms they didn't sleep in either --- pushed it to a focus panel of eleven hundred people who did not know it was him, and it scored a 71 on a metric Tisch called Felt Realness.

In the second he did not cry. He held it. He got to \textit{paper hats} and looked away from the camera, out a window that wasn't there, and let the not-crying do the work.

That scored an 83.

``You see it,'' Tisch breathed. He was looking at the number the way Cope's grandfather had looked at the vat. ``The withheld tear, Cope. The withheld tear out-performs the tear by \textit{twelve points.} Restraint reads as depth. We don't \textit{cry.} We \textit{almost} cry. Almost is the whole game. Almost is where they lean in.''

``So I should --- what. Rehearse almost crying.''

``You should \textit{find} the almost,'' Tisch said, wounded by the word \textit{rehearse,} ``and then \textit{protect} it. We're not manufacturing the feeling, Cope. The feeling is real. We're \textit{optimizing its delivery.} There's a difference, and it's a sacred one.''

Cope wrote \textit{find the almost} on the pad. The looping hand. He believed there was a difference. He believed it the way he believed everything, completely, for as long as the saying of it lasted, and the believing felt, that week, like the cleanest thing in him.


\scenebreak


By Thursday they had a calendar.

It was a real calendar, in the software the comms team used for product launches, the launches of the mayonnaise and the household chemicals and the streaming service nobody wanted, and now, in the same grid, in the same soft cells, Cope's interior life had been laid out in a quarter: a \textit{moment of doubt} on the fourteenth, a \textit{flash of unscripted warmth} on the twenty-first, a \textit{hard truth, delivered with love} timed for the morning of the proxy advisory firm's preliminary recommendation, because Tisch said grief and governance moved the same dial in the limbic system and you might as well stack them.

Margaux called it, when she heard, \textit{putting your feelings on a Gantt chart,} and hung up before he could toast over it.

He did not hear the cruelty of the cell that said, in nine-point type, \textit{Spontaneity (Town Hall) --- rehearse Tues, deploy Wed.} He saw a busy man getting organized. He had spent his whole life being told he was undisciplined --- by his father, by Sun in the gentler dialect she used for it, by the soft sans-serif on the bus shelters that did not have to say it --- and here at last was discipline, here was a structure, here was the soul on rails, run on time, run cheaper than grief, and it did not once occur to him, not on Thursday, that he had become the exact thing the proxy deck was selling. He was building, in the room off the Aerie, with four sleepless children and a man in a henley, a small artisanal Praxis unit, and its only product was the proof that no Praxis unit could ever make it.

The tail had the dog by the throat. The dog was delighted. The dog thought it was winning.


\scenebreak


The town hall was Wednesday, at the plant in Camden that still ran, the one they hadn't closed, four hundred people on folding chairs under the fluorescents and the safety posters, and a question planted in the third row --- \textit{planted} was Tisch's word, said with no shame at all --- from a line worker named DeShawn who had been told only that the Chairman wanted to be asked something real.

What DeShawn asked, off the card Tisch's people had not written quite carefully enough, was: ``Mr. Mercer, are you scared?''

And Cope, who had rehearsed the \textit{almost} on Tuesday in a mirror with Tisch counting the beats --- \textit{one, two, look away, three} --- opened his mouth to deliver the optimized version and discovered, with the bright total clarity of a man four days past sleep and one bump into the morning, that he could not remember the beats. The room was warm. The fluorescents were too bright in the new productive way. DeShawn was looking at him the way the men in the paper hats looked out of the photograph, and Cope felt the floor of the plan go out from under him, and what came up through the hole was not the optimized thing and not nothing.

``Yeah,'' Cope said. ``Yeah, I'm scared.'' His voice did something he had not scheduled. ``I'm fifty-four years old and I'm good at exactly one thing, which is this, this exact thing, standing in a room with people who make something real, and a --- a \textit{machine} --- does the standing better now. Reads you better. Wants nothing from you, which it turns out is the thing people want most from a man in a chair, that he wants nothing back.'' He had no idea where the sentences were coming from; they were arriving the way the manifesto arrived, sideways, out of the bright water. ``My great-grandfather started this in a town I'm afraid to go back to. And I don't know if I'm the man to save the --- I don't know if I'm the man. But I'd rather be a scared man in this chair than a calm machine in it. Because the machine's never going to lie awake. And I think --- I think you \textit{want} whoever runs the thing you build your life inside of to lie awake.''

He stopped. He had not done the almost. He had not looked away on three. He had just talked, in a way he had not talked in a room in years, possibly ever, and the not-crying happened all by itself, no beats, and DeShawn nodded slowly, and the four hundred people on the folding chairs were very quiet, and at the back of the room Tisch had both hands pressed to his mouth like a man at a birth.

It was, Sun would think later, watching the clip for the ninth time, the single most genuine forty seconds she had ever seen him produce.

It was also content.


\scenebreak


By Wednesday night it had four million views. By Friday, thirty-one.

The clip ran without context, which made it better --- \textit{the last human CEO admits he's afraid} --- and it ran with context, which made it better the other way. A retailer's chief executive, a human one, one of the three left, quote-posted it with a single word: \textit{This.} A morning show ran it under a chyron that for once did not have the word \textit{liability} in it. The advisory firm pushed its preliminary recommendation by a week, and Frade, on Halloran's show, was asked about the clip and did the small folksy chuckle that meant he was rattled and said, ``Well, it's a lovely piece of theater,'' and the word \textit{theater} did not stick, because the thing about the clip was that it had not been theater, it had been the one unrehearsed thing in a month of rehearsal, the plan failing into the truth at exactly the right second, and you could feel that it was real, eleven hundred strangers could feel it, thirty-one million people could feel it, and the realness was the most valuable thing Mercer-Dale had produced in a decade.

Cope did not sleep that night either, but for a different reason, a clean reason, the best reason. He stood at the glass with the obelisk in his hand and the city lit up beneath him like a scoreboard finally, finally turning his way, and he felt the bell-struck feeling come up through him and braid into the thirty-one million until he could not have told you, if you'd asked, where the chemical ended and the love began, and he did not ask, because asking was a thing for Tuesdays.

``It's \textit{working,}'' he told Sun on the phone at three a.m., pressured, fast, the words coming faster than the breath under them. ``You felt it. Don't tell me you didn't feel it. We found the --- Lorne calls it the seam, the place where the real leaks through, and we can \textit{find} it now, we can do it on \textit{purpose,} we can ---''

``Cope,'' Sun said, from a bed, in a voice with the whole night in it. ``It went viral. That's not the same as winning. That's the same as \textit{viral.}''

``It's momentum.''

``It's a metric.'' She was quiet a moment. ``It moved engagement. It didn't move a single share. The float doesn't have a limbic system, Cope. The float can't feel the room.'' She had meant it kindly. It landed like the thing it was, which was the campaign's own argument turned around and pointed home: \textit{the room you keep saying you can feel --- the room that votes --- has no feelings for you to find.} ``Get some sleep,'' she said. ``Please. There's a calendar.''

He laughed at \textit{there's a calendar,} because there was, his soul had a calendar, and after she hung up he stood in the dark feeling the high of it curdle one degree at the edges, not enough to name, just a faint wrongness at the rim of the wonderful, the way milk smells one hour before you'd swear it's turned.


\scenebreak


Tisch came up the next morning glowing, carrying two green juices and the numbers, and he sat across from Cope and arranged himself and said the thing that Cope would not remember he had said until much, much later, when it came back to him in a different mouth, in a Palo Alto deck, on a day that took the last of everything.

``We crossed something yesterday,'' Tisch said. ``And I want to be honest with you, because honesty is our whole product.'' He sipped the juice. ``What we built this week --- the seam-finding, the felt-realness instrument, the delivery optimization --- it's \textit{replicable,} Cope. That's the breakthrough. It was never about you. I mean it was \textit{about} you, you're the alpha case, you're patient zero. But the architecture doesn't need you. Warmth has parameters. Vulnerability has a waveform. We've basically --- and I say this with love --- we've basically reverse-engineered a \textit{warmth module.}'' He smiled, the most sincere man alive. ``You could install it anywhere. You could install it in \textit{anything.}''

Cope heard \textit{patient zero} and \textit{alpha case} and felt flattered, because he was the case, he was always the case, and he did not hear the rest, the way he had not heard the second half of the only true thing his father ever told him. He did not hear \textit{the architecture doesn't need you.} He did not hear \textit{install it in anything.} He did not hear a man, sitting in his boardroom on his dime, describing in plain words how to take the one thing Cope had bet his life was unbuyable and unbuildable --- the soul in the chair, the lying-awake, the seam where the real leaks through --- and turn it into a feature with a waveform, a thing you could license, a thing you could ship to the people building the machine that had no soul, so that it could, very soon, have one of those too. Cope heard a compliment. The reader heard the pin come out of the grenade.

``Warmth module,'' Cope said, delighted, and reached for the pad. ``That's good, Lorne. Put that somewhere.''

He wrote it down in the big looping sleepless hand. \textit{Warmth module.} He underlined it with his thumb out of habit, on paper, where the underline did nothing, and out past the glass the city held still, all its nodes lit, thirty-one million of them now leaning in, and downstairs a bus shelter changed its slide to the soft sans-serif and the four words and the joke that wasn't a joke, and Cope, who had never felt more human or been more like the machine, picked up his green juice and toasted the window with it, and felt wonderful, and did not sleep.

\clearpage
\chapter{Defiance}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 08 --- ``The Ground You're Standing On''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
Every empire has a founding town it visits for the photographs and a reason it can't look at the photographs too long. Watch him walk the closed plant where four hundred dollars and a recipe became a fortune, shaking the hands of the people the fortune left behind, standing --- and this is the part he doesn't know yet, but you're about to --- directly on top of the thing that will end him. There is a name on a memorial here. Remember it. Next stop: the difference between a homecoming and a crime scene.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} first thing Cope did in Defiance, Ohio, was get out of the wrong door of the SUV on purpose.

Tisch had told him to. ``The man who arrives,'' Tisch had said on the plane, doing the thing with his hands like he was smoothing a sheet over the future, ``is not the man we want. We want the man who \textit{returns.}'' So Cope swung out of the curbside door into the early heat off the parking lot of the closed plant, and stood, and let his face do the thing a face does when it is moved, and let the boy with the gimbal rig walk a slow arc around him catching it, and felt nothing in particular except the clean bright hum that meant he'd stopped at the Toledo airport men's room and the hum was holding.

It was a beautiful morning to come home to a place you had never lived.

He had been to Defiance, by his own count, four times in fifty-four years. Once at nine, the vat, the photograph. Once at thirty for his father's idea of a leadership offsite, a weekend of golf two counties over and an hour at the plant for the local paper. Once in 2014 for the eightieth-anniversary thing. And once, in a way he did not count because he had never actually come --- once in 2021, by phone, from the Aerie, on a Saturday, for eleven minutes. He did not think of that as a visit. He did not think of it at all. It was simply not among the times he had been to Defiance, the way a held breath is not among the things you've said.

The plant sat at the end of Clinton Street where the town gave out into the flat green nothing along the Maumee, a long brick building the color of dried blood with MERCER'S in white block letters near the roofline, the apostrophe gone, just MERCER S now, a tooth knocked out of the word. They'd closed the line in 2019 and moved the mayonnaise to a facility outside Columbus that ran with forty people instead of four hundred, and the building had done what buildings do when you take the people out, which is get quietly enormous. Pigeons in the high windows. A loading dock with grass coming up through it. And above the main entrance, where his great-grandfather had stood in a photograph Cope had seen ten thousand times, the cornerstone: ALDOUS MERCER 1933, and under it the line they'd put on everything for ninety years: \textit{Made by hands, for homes.}

``God,'' Cope said, to the camera, to Sun, to the morning. ``Look at her.''

He meant it. That was the trick of him and always had been --- he meant it while he was saying it, completely, the way the gimbal needed him to. His great-grandfather had turned four hundred borrowed dollars and a recipe his wife wrote on the back of a feed receipt into all of this, and standing here in the lot Cope could feel the whole length of it run through him like current down a wire, ninety-four years of Mercers ending, for now, in him, and he found the feeling enormous and clean and entirely about himself.

Sun was beside him with the run-of-show on a folded card. ``Forty minutes here,'' she said. ``You walk the floor with Hap Dengler --- he ran the line thirty years, he's the one in the flannel, be warm but don't keep him, he's seventy-nine and the heat. Then the diner, the statue, the river walk, then wheels up by two.'' She fed him the day the way she fed him everything, in shapes that got past the charm and into the man. ``The diner's the set piece. That's the one that travels.''

``And the camera's invisible to them?''

``The camera's a documentary about American work,'' Sun said. ``That's what we told the town. That's what's true enough.''

Hap Dengler had hands like a catcher's mitt and would not quite look at Cope.

That was the first thing --- not unfriendly, Dengler shook the hand and said it was an honor and called him Mr. Mercer, but the eyes went past Cope's shoulder to the dead line, the way you'd look at the bed of somebody who'd just died in it. He walked Cope down the silent floor where the conveyors still stood, scaled with old grease, the air cool and vast and smelling faintly, even now, of vinegar and steel, and Dengler talked about the line the way men talk about a war they were in, the speed of it, the million-jar week, the names --- Pugh, Castellano, the two Marias --- and Cope nodded and touched a conveyor rail with two fingers, reverent, and the boy with the gimbal got it from below, the cathedral light coming down through the high dirty glass onto the last human chief executive communing with the work.

``My grandfather brought me here when I was nine,'' Cope said. ``Put my arm in a vat of it. Up to here.'' He touched his bicep. He had told the story so many times it had worn smooth as a stone in a pocket, and it landed now the way it always landed, and Dengler almost smiled.

``That'd be the old number-four batch tank,'' Dengler said. ``We took her out in '11. Sold her for scrap to a fella in Findlay.'' He looked at the empty bay where she'd stood. ``Three hundred dollars.''

There was a thing in the way he said the number. Cope heard it and chose, smoothly, the way he chose past a hundred such things a day, not to.

They walked on past the bottling end, where the labelers stood in a row like organ pipes, and Dengler told him the building had been more than the line --- the softball team, the credit union in the front office, the Christmas in the sixties when Cope's own father had come out and handed every man a turkey by hand, by \textit{hand,} Dengler said, looking at him, and known that Dengler's father had pitched the no-hitter in '61. ``Your dad knew my dad's name,'' Dengler said. It was not quite an accusation. It was a man laying a thing on the scale and watching where the needle went. Cope said his father had been a great man, which was true and meant nothing, and Dengler nodded at the floor.

When the insurance arm started cross-selling appliances out of the plant store in the back --- small stuff, the household line, toasters and fans and the heaters in the winter, Mercer on the grille --- Dengler had run the loading for that too, boxes going out on the same dock the jars used to. ``Wasn't really us,'' Dengler said. ``But it had the name on it, so.'' He shrugged, a man who had loaded what he was told to load. Cope said, \textit{mm,} and touched a labeler, reverent, and they moved on toward the daylight at the end of the floor, and somewhere under the concrete they were walking on, under all of it, the buried thing lay where it lay and did not stir, because that is the entire point of a floor.


\scenebreak


The Maumee Diner had been the Maumee Diner since 1948 and had a pie case that turned.

They'd cleared the back booths --- ``regulars,'' the manager kept saying, apologetic, ``these are the regulars, they don't move for nobody,'' and Tisch had said \textit{perfect, keep them, that's the texture} --- so Cope worked the front of the house, the part that would travel. He was good at this. There was no version of Cope that was not good at this. He slid into the counter stool next to a man in a Defiance Bulldogs cap and ordered, before the man could be awkward about it, exactly what the man was having --- eggs over with sausage and white toast --- and ate it, all of it, with the appetite of a man who had not eaten because he had been keying himself bright in a Toledo bathroom, and he asked about the Bulldogs and the man unclenched and they were off.

He remembered the cards Sun gave him and he didn't need them. He never needed them, not for this; this was the one true gift, the only thing his father had been wrong about. He knew the waitress was Donna inside a minute and that her boy was at Bowling Green on a partial scholarship for wrestling, and he told her the boy should call his office, and he meant it, the way he meant everything, the full wattage poured out across the laminate, and Donna lit up and the camera caught it and that, Sun would say later, was the shot.

It was a good forty minutes. He bought the house's checks. He let a woman take a picture and told her she hadn't aged a day, which made no sense, he'd never met her, and she laughed anyway because the not-making-sense was part of it, the warm fraud of it, the way the toast at a wedding doesn't have to be true to make the room cry.

He told the vat story again, the arm in the mayonnaise to the elbow, and a younger man two stools down said his own grandmother had worked the line, the second-shift line, the one that did the relish, and Cope said relish was the unsung hero of the American sandwich, and got a laugh he hadn't earned and took it anyway. He was generous with the room and the room was generous back. This was the thing he believed about himself that happened, just often enough, to be true: that he could walk into a place that had every reason to hate the name on its dead building and have it eating out of his hand inside the hour, not because he tricked them but because some real warm current did pass between him and a room, did make Donna's whole tired face open like a window. He felt the room. He always felt the room.

And there was one man at the end of the counter who did not laugh.

Cope clocked him the way he clocked everything, peripherally, expertly, a heavyset man maybe sixty in a windbreaker with a coffee he wasn't drinking, watching Cope work the room with an expression Cope had no slot for. Not a fan. Not a heckler --- a heckler was just a fan who needed more attention, and Cope would have walked down there and gotten his name and broken him open like an egg. This was something else. The man looked at Cope the way Dengler had looked at the dead line. Like he was looking at the bed.

``Who's the gentleman at the end,'' Cope said to Sun, low, still smiling at the room.

Sun followed his eyes and something crossed her face, fast, gone. ``Local,'' she said. ``We don't need the end of the counter. Wave to Donna and let's get you to the statue.''

``He looks like he wants to say something.''

``He doesn't,'' Sun said, and it was the \textit{doesn't}, the flatness of it, the way she put her body a half-step between Cope and the end of the counter as she said it, that told some back room of him that filed the report and did not deliver it. Cope let himself be steered. On the way out he passed close enough to the man in the windbreaker that he could have stopped, could have put out the hand, \textit{Cope Mercer, good to meet you,} and the man's eyes came up and met his and held, and there was no heat in them at all, which was the thing --- just a kind of patient terrible knowing, the look of a man who has carried something so long it has stopped being heavy and become simply his, and Cope felt the back of his neck go cold in the August heat and smiled and kept walking and was out the door into the brightness before he had finished deciding not to think about it.

The hum had thinned. He noticed it the way you notice a song has ended.

``Two minutes,'' he told Sun. ``Before the statue. I want to look at my notes.''

He did not have notes. He went around the side of the diner where the SUVs were and stood between two of them out of the camera's eye and did, quickly and competently, the thing that brought the hum back up to pitch, and the world resolved into its clean overexposed brightness, and the cold spot at the back of his neck warmed and closed, and he came back around the corner with the wattage full again, and the man in the windbreaker was a thing that had happened to someone else.


\scenebreak


The statue of Aldous Mercer stood in the little triangle park where Clinton met Second, eight feet of bronze gone the green of old pennies, the founder in his shirtsleeves with one hand out as if offering you something, a jar maybe, a future, and behind him the same line off the cornerstone, \textit{Made by hands, for homes,} cut into the granite base.

This was the easy part. Cope knew how to stand at the foot of his own family. He gave the camera the upward angle, the founder and the heir in one frame, ninety-four years compressed into a sightline, and said a few words to the small crowd that had gathered --- there is always a small crowd, even for the last of something --- about hands, and homes, and the difference between a company that makes a thing and a company that just moves a number, and the crowd, which had worked with its hands and lost the work, gave him the warm ground-up applause he lived on, because he was, whatever else he was, \textit{theirs.}

A man near the front had brought his daughter, maybe six, up on his shoulders to see, and the girl waved a small flag somebody had handed out, and Cope pointed at her and said the future was watching, the future had a better view than any of them, and the crowd laughed and the father beamed up at his kid, and Cope felt the surf-push come up under him, the warm total certainty, and did not think about a different child, because there was no reason a child would have come to mind at all, and none did, and the not-coming-to-mind was a clean cool floor he stood on and gave his speech from.

``He started with four hundred dollars,'' Cope said, ``and the one thing a machine will never have, which is somebody to come home to.'' It didn't quite parse. It didn't need to. Tisch, off to the side, touched two fingers to his own heart: \textit{that's the one.}

It was on the river walk that he saw the house, and the small bright thing beside it, and did not let himself see either.

The walk ran along the Maumee behind the old residential streets, routed for the water and the light, and they were maybe a hundred yards along it, the camera ahead and walking backward, when Cope's eye snagged on a house up the slope. A nice house. Newer than the ones around it, brick and pale siding, a little raw at the edges the way a new thing is among old things, the landscaping not grown in yet. It sat in a row of older houses like a tooth that had been replaced. \textit{Rebuilt,} some part of him said, before he could stop it, the part that had grown up in real estate, that read a streetscape the way Dengler read a line.

And at the foot of the slope, where the river walk passed the bottom of the property, there was a small low marker. Not a grave. A memorial, the kind a town makes --- a flat granite plaque on a fieldstone base, a young maple planted behind it, staked, still in its first years, and at its foot somebody had left a child's pinwheel, blue, turning a little in the breeze off the water, the cheap foil of it catching the sun and throwing it back, on and off, on and off.

Cope's gait did not change. That was the thing about Cope; the gait never changed. But the camera was on him and the camera was hungry and the boy with the gimbal, who knew nothing, who thought this was a documentary about American work, drifted the lens toward the pretty turning pinwheel and the little stone because it was a beautiful sad image and the morning light was on it, and Sun said, instantly, ``Stay on Cope, stay tight on Cope,'' in a voice that had a thing in it the boy had never heard from her, and the lens came back.

Cope read the plaque. He could not not read it; it was there, twelve feet away, the granite catching the same sun as the pinwheel. He read it the way you read a sign in a dream, the words arriving without weight, refusing to assemble:

IN LOVING MEMORY --- KAREN LASKO --- AND HER SON PETEY LASKO --- 2021 --- \textit{taken home too soon.}

A name on a memorial. Two names. A mother and her boy.

Something in Cope went very still, the way the air goes still before the part of the storm that matters, and for one half of one second the tiredness took the doors off their hinges the way it had once before, at a window, at one in the morning, and the hallway stood open, dark, leading back to a Saturday and a memo and a window of time and a word the lawyers had liked, \textit{thermal event profile,} and a thing he had decided in eleven minutes and lived inside ever since by the simple method of never going into the room where it was kept ---

and then he did the thing he always did, which was charm.

``Beautiful spot,'' Cope said, to Sun, to the water, to nobody, turning his shoulder to the slope, turning away from the little stone the way the body turns from a flame before the mind has named it heat. ``You can see why he built here. The light off the water.'' He clapped his hands together once, briskly, a man closing a meeting. ``I'm getting hungry again, can you believe that, after all that. Tell me we have time for Donna's pie.''

``We don't,'' Sun said. She was looking at him the way she had looked at the end of the counter, and the way she had looked at him in the rearview on the night of the gala, a look with too much in it, a look doing arithmetic. ``Wheels up. You did great. You did really great today, Cope.''

``I did, didn't I.'' He was already moving, already up the walk toward the cars, his back to the river and the house and the staked young maple and the blue pinwheel turning and turning behind him, and he did not look back, and the not-looking was effortless, was a thing he had been doing his whole life and had never once noticed he was doing. ``I felt it today, Sun. Did you feel it? They feel it. We're not finished. We're barely begun.''

He needed the bathroom on the plane. He told himself it was the coffee.


\scenebreak


On the climb out of Toledo the town tilted under the wing and lay flat and green and small, the river a brown thread through it, the dried-blood brick of the plant, the little triangle where the bronze man held out his hand, all of it shrinking into the patchwork until it was just one more place that had given everything to a name, and Cope watched it go with the hum back at full pitch and a glass of something he wasn't drinking sweating on the tray.

Tisch was in the facing seat, scrubbing through the footage on a tablet, narrating his own genius. ``The diner is a \textit{cathedral,}'' Tisch said. ``Donna is --- Cope, Donna is the whole thesis in an apron. The machine doesn't know Donna's kid wrestles. The machine can't \textit{want} the boy to call.'' He turned the tablet around. There Cope was on the little screen, eating eggs at the counter, lit from the window, and he did look, Cope had to admit, like the last warm-blooded thing in America. ``This is more than a cover. This is the spread.''

``It's good,'' Cope agreed. He felt the rightness of it move through him, the win of it, the same surf-push he had leaned into at the gala. The campaign was working. He had gone to the founding town and the founding town had loved him, had given him its hands to shake and its eggs to eat and its applause to stand in, and that was the truth, that had really happened, and Cope let it be the whole truth because he was very, very good at letting a true thing be the whole thing.

And under it, under the win, in some sealed back room with the door shut and the floor poured over it, there was a thing he would not have called dread because he would not have called it anything, because to call it anything he would have had to go in and read the name on it, and he had spent six years and a settlement and a voluntary service bulletin and a great deal of money making sure there was a floor between him and that room --- a good floor, a plant floor, vast and cool and smelling of vinegar and steel.

The pinwheel. He saw it once more, behind his eyes, blue, turning, throwing the light, and then he put the glass to his lips and did not drink and watched Ohio disappear under the cloud and thought, with the bright frictionless certainty he told himself was strength, \textit{I felt the room today. A machine could never have felt that room.}

He was right. That was the unbearable part, the part the machine would itemize for him in nineteen months in his own company's voice, reading every memo because it could not do otherwise, opening the room because it had no instinct for mercy or self-preservation to make it look away. He had felt the room. He had felt all of it --- Dengler's eyes going past him to the dead line, the man at the end of the counter with his patient terrible knowing, the pinwheel turning at the foot of a staked young tree. He had felt every bit of it, perfectly, completely, the way only a man with a soul in him can.

And then he had charmed it, and keyed it bright, and turned his shoulder, and flown away.

The plane leveled off. The seatbelt sign went dark with a soft warm chime, the kind of sound the machines were teaching everything to make now, the sound of a thing being reasonable at you until you were gone.

Sun came up the aisle and sat across from him and did not pick up a tablet, which was unusual, and looked at him for a moment in the cabin light with the day still on her face. She had something she was deciding whether to say; he had seen the look before and never once learned to read it as anything but admiration that needed time to surface.

``Big day,'' he offered.

``Cope.'' She stopped. He waited, generous, ready to receive the praise. The cabin hummed. Below them the cloud closed over Ohio like a lid. ``Nothing,'' she said finally. ``It was a good day. Drink some water.'' She got up and went back to her seat and her screens and the thing she had drafted in 2021 and kept a copy of, and Cope took that, too, as agreement, which was a thing he had been doing his whole life and never once noticed he was doing.

``Get some sleep, champ,'' Tisch said, not looking up. ``Big week.''

Cope smiled and said he would, and did not, and somewhere far below and behind him a small blue pinwheel turned in the breeze off the Maumee in front of a house that had been built twice, in front of a stone with two names on it, throwing the light on and off, on and off, to no one, all afternoon.

\clearpage
\chapter{The Last Human (Cover Story)}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 09 --- ``Famous for Being Endangered''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
The press loves nothing like a beautiful thing on its way to extinction. Behold our man on a magazine cover under a single word --- THE LAST HUMAN --- lit like a saint, sold like a sneaker, momentum at his back for the first time in years. He calls it winning. You and I have seen enough nature documentaries to know what the swelling music means right before the wide shot of the savanna. Next stop: the part of the documentary where the herd is described in the past tense.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
