\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} lighting director had named the look, and the name was \textit{grace.}

He said it twice, the lighting director, a narrow young man in head-to-toe black who moved around the studio floor like a priest finding his marks, \textit{we're going to give him grace, give him a little grace from camera left,} and a softbox the size of a barn door swung in on a boom and warmed one half of Cope's face to gold and left the other in a clean blue shadow, and Cope, on the apple box they'd stood him on without quite explaining why, felt the warmth come across his jaw like the sun coming over the back nine, and understood that he was, at fifty-four, in the best light of his life.

They had taken over a daylight studio in a converted bakery in Bushwick, twelve thousand square feet of white cyclorama and rigging, and there were, by Cope's loose and delighted count, nineteen people there to photograph one man. A photographer flown in from London who shot only on film and said almost nothing and was paid, Sun had told him in the car with no expression at all, more per day than a line worker in Defiance had made in a year. Two assistants for the photographer and two for the assistants. A digital tech at a tethered laptop in the corner, a ``DIT,'' who said \textit{firing} before every frame like a man calling a horse race. The lighting director and his acolytes. A stylist with a rolling rack of fourteen near-identical navy suits, every one of them his size, none of them his. A groomer who came at his face between every setup with a little folding brush and a tin of something and worked the shine off his forehead with a tenderness no one had shown his forehead in years. A producer with a clipboard and a headset. And, off in the gloom past the lights, in a hard plastic chair she had pulled to exactly the wrong angle so that she could watch the monitor and watch him at the same time, the writer.

Her name was Delia March and she had been with him for two days and was going to be with him, the contract said, for six weeks.

``Chin down a hair, up to me with the eyes,'' the photographer said, the longest sentence he had produced all morning, and Cope gave him the chin and gave him the eyes, and somewhere in the rafters the strobes went off with a sound like a small clean catastrophe, \textit{whump,} and a wash of white, and the room held its breath the way rooms do around a man being made into an image, and Cope held the look a beat past the flash the way the camera always wanted, and felt marvelous, and felt the hum holding under it all like a tone you stop hearing because it never stops.

He had done a discreet amount in the car. He thought of it now the way he had started thinking of it lately, which was not as the thing it was but as the thing it did: a discipline. A pre-shot routine. Every athlete had one. His grandfather had taken two fingers of rye before the annual meeting for forty years and called it \textit{settling the hands,} and no one had ever called Aldous Mercer an addict, they had called him a titan, and the difference between a titan and an addict, Cope had decided somewhere over Pennsylvania this spring, was outcomes. You did not get to be the last warm-blooded thing in the American economy by accident. You got there on edge, on appetite, on the refusal to let the body's small treasons --- the want of sleep, the gray hour, the sag at four in the afternoon --- set the terms of the campaign. The machine never sagged. Fine. Neither, this week, did he. He had simply, he told himself, found his own clean.

''\textit{Firing,}'' said the DIT.

\textit{Whump.}


\scenebreak


The single word had been Delia March's idea, which was the first thing about her that frightened the part of Cope that did not get frightened.

She had pitched it on day one, in the Aerie, sitting in the same hard angle to him she favored everywhere, notebook on her knee and a recorder on the table she had asked permission for in a way that made the permission feel like she was doing him the favor. \textit{The cover's one word,} she had said. \textit{Not your name. Everyone knows your name, that's the problem, your name's a logo. One word, white on black. THE LAST HUMAN.} And Cope had felt the rightness of it land in him like a putt dropping, and had said \textit{yes, God, yes, that's it,} and Tisch, who had been hovering, had touched two fingers to his heart, and only later, alone at the glass at two in the morning with the city lit up like a circuit diagram of his own genius, had Cope turned the phrase over the way he'd turned the obelisk and seen the city break apart through it. \textit{The last human.} The last. He had said it a hundred times into a hundred rooms as a banner, a battle cry, and only at two a.m. did some back room of him file the quieter report, that \textit{last} is a word you say about a thing that is ending, that you do not put the word \textit{last} on a beginning, and that Delia March had handed him a coronation and an obituary in the same three syllables and watched his face to see which one he'd hear.

He had heard the coronation. He always heard the coronation.

But he had clocked her watching. He had a sense for the watching. He had spent thirty years being the most watched man in any room and had developed, the way a fish develops a lateral line, an instinct for the difference between the watching that wanted something from him and the watching that wanted \textit{him,} and Delia March's watching was a third kind he did not have a slot for, a watching that wanted to \textit{understand} him, which was so much more dangerous than either of the others that he found, to his alarm and delight, that he could not stop performing for it.

She was forty, maybe forty-two, with a flat unhurried way of holding eye contact and a habit of writing while she looked at you, so you never knew which thing you'd just said was the thing she'd written down. She wore good boots and bad coats. She had won the kind of award that gets a profile writer flown in on film budgets, for a piece --- Sun had read it to him in the car, dry, \textit{here's who you're dealing with} --- about a senator who had cried in front of her and resigned within the year, not because of anything in the piece, just because the piece had been so exactly \textit{true} about him that he could no longer un-see himself. \textit{She doesn't do hatchet jobs,} Sun had said, folding the phone away. \textit{That's the thing about her. She does autopsies, and the subject's alive for it, and they thank her.}

``That should worry me,'' Cope had said.

``It worries me,'' Sun had said, which was as close as Sun came, these days, to begging.


\scenebreak


They broke for lunch and Delia March took her sandwich to the wrong chair instead of the catering table and Cope, who could not pass a person sitting alone without trying to fix the aloneness onto himself, took his to the chair beside her.

``You're not eating with the cool kids,'' he said.

``I'm working.'' She did not look up from her notebook. ``You're the cool kids.''

``Am I.'' He liked that. He turned it over. ``Cope Mercer, the cool kids.'' He bit the sandwich, which was extraordinary, some kind of cured thing on bread that cost what a sandwich costs in a neighborhood that used to make bread for people who worked, and he was suddenly, ravenously hungry, the bright clean want that came up under the hum and that he had stopped, this spring, calling by its other name. ``Ask me something. You've got that face. You've been saving one up all morning.''

Delia March looked at him then, fully, the way she did, and he felt the warmth of it land where the softbox had, on one half of him, and leave the other in shadow.

``Why does everyone call you Cope,'' she said.

It was not the question he'd braced for. He'd braced for the machine, for Frade, for the 13D, for the numbers, for \textit{aren't you afraid,} and he'd had the answers loaded and warm. He had not braced for the name.

``My nanny,'' he said. ``I was three. Prescott's a lot of name for three. Coppy, then Cope.'' He gave her the version he gave everyone, the smooth stone of it. ``Stuck.''

``Cope,'' she said, and wrote something, looking at him. ``As in, \textit{to cope.}''

``As in \textit{to cope.}'' He smiled, the whole smile. ``I'm named for getting through it, Delia. Whatever \textit{it} is. The family motto, basically. Made by hands, for homes, and the kid copes.''

``Does he,'' she said. Not a question. She let it sit there in the studio between them, the strobes ticking as they recharged, \textit{to cope,} and Cope felt, for the second time since he'd met her, the specific vertigo of having handed someone the truth dressed as a charm and watched them quietly take the dress off and hang it up. He laughed. He always laughed; the laugh was a door he could close. But he was aware, biting the extraordinary sandwich, of having lost a small exchange he had not known they were having, and of wanting --- this was the part that frightened the unfrightenable part of him --- of \textit{wanting} to lose the next one to her too.

``Eat your lunch, champ,'' he said, which made no sense, she was younger than him but not by enough for \textit{champ,} and she heard the not-making-sense and wrote it down, smiling, and he saw her write it and felt the warmth come over his jaw again, grace, from camera left, and thought: \textit{six weeks.}


\scenebreak


The shot they ended on was the cover and everyone in the room knew it the way a room knows a closing argument.

They had killed the navy suits. The stylist had put him, at the photographer's grunt, into a plain white shirt, no tie --- Cope had balked at the no-tie, \textit{I'm the last man in America who wears a tie, that's the whole story,} and the photographer had said, the second-longest sentence of the day, \textit{that's why we take it off,} and Cope had felt the cold rightness of that go through him and shut up. They had stripped the cyclorama to black. They had brought the grace light down to a single source, high and to the left, so that it fell on him from above the way the cathedral light had come down through the dirty glass at the Defiance plant, and the room had gone quiet, all nineteen of them, the producer's headset chatter stilled, the DIT not even saying \textit{firing,} and the photographer had come out from behind the camera and walked all the way up to Cope on his apple box and looked at him for a long moment from two feet away with no expression at all.

``Stop selling,'' the photographer said, very quietly, just to him. ``There's nobody to sell. Everybody's gone home. It's just you, and you're the last one, and you know it.'' And he walked back to the camera in the dark.

And something in Cope --- the hum, the tiredness with the doors off its hinges, the two a.m. report about the word \textit{last,} the pinwheel he had not thought about in eleven days, all of it --- something let go of the face, just for a second, just for the length of a held breath, and what was left on him was not the toast and not the wattage but the thing underneath, the man on the box in the white shirt in the high cold light who had been given a trophy for going extinct and had carried it up to a dark office at one in the morning because he did not have anywhere else to put it down.

\textit{Whump.}

``That's it,'' the photographer said. ``We're done. Thank you.'' And he started, immediately, to break down his own camera, the way the great ones do, the work over the second it's over, and the room exhaled and began to move, and from her chair in the dark Delia March did not move at all, and did not write anything, and looked at Cope on the box with an expression he finally, fatally, did have a slot for, and the slot it fit was the one marked \textit{seen.}

He stepped down off the apple box and the groomer came at his forehead and the producer said wonderful, wonderful, and Sun appeared at his elbow out of the architecture with a bottle of water and a look on her face that had thirty thousand miles on it.

``You good,'' Sun said. It was not quite a question.

``I'm the cool kids,'' Cope said.


\scenebreak


The car back was just the two of them, and Sun was tired, and Cope was electric, which was the usual division of labor, and he talked the whole way down the BQE about the photographer, about the grace light, about the no-tie, \textit{take it off, do you get it, Sun, take off the one thing that --- that's a genius, that man's a genius,} and Sun watched the river of brake lights and let him run because letting him run was easier than the alternative and she had stopped, somewhere this spring, choosing the alternative.

``She asked me why they call me Cope,'' he said, somewhere over the bridge.

``Delia March.''

``She wrote it down. \textit{To cope.} She --- '' He stopped. He did not have the word for what Delia March had done, or rather he had the word and would not say it in a car, the way he would not say a different name in a car, the way you do not say in a car the things you most need to say. ``She's good,'' he said instead. ``She's really good. We're lucky to have her on it.''

``We don't have her,'' Sun said. The car went down off the bridge into the lit canyon of it. ``That's the thing you keep getting wrong about her, Cope. We don't have her. She has us.'' She turned and looked at him in the passing orange, her glasses up in her hair, and she said it in three parts because she had trained him to receive things in threes, because it was the only shape that ever got past the charm and into the man. ``She's not on the team. She's not running comms. She's writing six thousand words about whether a human being should run a company, and you are her evidence, and right now you cannot tell the difference between being \textit{understood} and being \textit{studied,} and that --- '' the third part landed flat and quiet, a stone laid on a scale --- ``that is exactly the difference Frade is paying eleven million dollars to make the whole country stop being able to tell.''

Cope heard about a third of it. The good third, the part with \textit{six thousand words} in it, the part that meant he mattered, that meant the campaign was working, that meant the banner was up and the press was saluting it. He had his phone out, the screen too bright in the dark car, thumbing too fast, and he was already three turns ahead, already at the newsstand, already at the cover, white on black, the one word, the grace light, the last warm-blooded thing in America.

``It's working,'' he said. ``Sun. Look at me. \textit{It's working.} When's the last time we had a week like this? When's the last time we had \textit{momentum?}'' And he laughed, the bell-struck feeling ringing through him clean and high and total, the one he had been calling, all spring, \textit{winning,} and outside a bus shelter slid by with a Praxis ad on it, just the wordmark and the soft sans-serif of the people who would be reasonable at you until you were gone, \textit{Leadership as a service,} and under it the joke that wasn't a joke, \textit{No off-sites,} and Cope did not see it, because he was looking at his phone, at a mock of the cover Tisch had already had built, at his own face in the high cold light under the word LAST, and he thought it was the most beautiful thing he had ever seen, and in a sense he was right.

``Drop me at the office,'' he said. ``I think better up high.''

Sun looked at him in the mirror for a second too long, the way she had on the night of the gala and on the plane out of Toledo, a look with too much in it, a look doing arithmetic, and then she told the driver Sixth Avenue, because the alternative was harder and she had stopped, this spring, choosing the alternative, and Cope took the not-fighting as agreement, which was a thing he had been doing his whole life and had never once noticed he was doing.


\scenebreak


The magazine hit the newsstands eleven days later, on a Tuesday, and Cope, who had not slept Monday in any way a doctor would sign off on, walked down to the corner himself at six in the morning to see it in the wild.

He did this. He told no one. He put on a cap, which fooled nobody and everybody, and he went down the forty-one floors and out past the night man into the gray scrubbed early light of Sixth Avenue, and he walked two blocks south to the one newsstand left on the avenue, a metal box of a thing run by a man named Yusuf who had been there since before the machines and would, Cope thought, be there after, selling gum and lottery and the last printed proof that anything had happened, and there, in the rack at eye level, between a fashion book and a thing about houses, was his own face.

White on black. The grace light coming down from the high left. The white shirt, no tie. The eyes up to camera, past selling, just the man, the last one, who knew it. And across the bottom, where the photographer had stripped everything else away, the single word, in a typeface clean and warm and frictionless as the future itself:

THE LAST HUMAN.

Cope stood on the cold sidewalk in his stupid cap and looked at himself looking back, and the bell-struck feeling came up through the soles of his feet and filled him to the crown, total, clean, high, and he was, in that moment, on that corner, as happy as he had been since he was nine years old with his arm in a vat to the elbow and a row of men in paper hats behind him whose names he had known once and lost. He bought five copies. He bought all five Yusuf had. He overpaid and would not take the change and asked Yusuf his grandson's name and meant it, completely, for ninety seconds, and Yusuf, who did not have a grandson, said thank you Mr. Mercer and put the money in the drawer.

``They love me,'' Cope said, to Yusuf, to the avenue, to the gray light and the scrubbed empty early city and the five copies of his own extinction warm against his chest. ``Yusuf. They love me. We're not finished. We're barely begun.''

And the avenue, which was empty, said nothing back, and far uptown a board's wife at her own breakfast turned a printed page and did the small private arithmetic that boards' wives do, and somewhere in Palo Alto a server farm that felt nothing and wanted nothing and could not be charmed or shamed or loved ran its dull diligent overnight pass through every memo Mercer-Dale had ever filed, patient, tireless, reading a Saturday in 2021 the way it read everything, completely and for use, because it could not do otherwise, and out in front of a rebuilt house in Defiance a blue pinwheel turned in the dark before dawn, throwing no light, because there was no light yet to throw, and Cope Mercer walked back up his own avenue in the cold with five copies of the cover held to his heart like a man carrying something home, and felt wonderful, and did not sleep, and was, the picture was right about this, the picture was right about all of it, the last of something.

\clearpage
\chapter{The Listening Tour}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 10 --- ``Discipline, He Calls It''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
The banner needs feeding and so does he. Watch him run an unscripted tour of his own goodness with a journalist embedded close enough to become the next entry in a pattern he experiences as generosity. The powder is discipline now. The affair is \textit{really seeing people.} Every appetite the machine doesn't have, he is indulging in the exact week he campaigns on having a soul the machine lacks. Next stop: the place where the cure and the disease share a hotel key.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} tour was unscripted, which meant it had taken nine days to script.

It was Tisch's idea and then, within an hour of being Tisch's idea, it was Cope's idea, the way good ideas migrated in the Aerie, crossing the rug from the man in the henley to the man at the desk and arriving sourceless and warm, like weather. \textit{The Last Human Listens.} That was the working title, until legal flagged that you could not trademark \textit{listens,} and then it was just the Listening Tour, six cities in eight days, no podium, no prepared remarks, the Chairman of Mercer-Dale simply \textit{out among the people,} hearing them, holding their hands, being --- Tisch's word, said with two fingers on his own sternum --- \textit{available.}

The people were pre-vetted. The hands had signed releases.

``Spontaneity,'' Tisch had said on the first plane, ``is a \textit{production value.} The most expensive thing in the world to make look free.'' And so there was a manifest, and the manifest had columns, and one of the columns was named, in the comms team's product-launch software, \textit{Unscripted Beat,} and under it, for Tuesday, in nine-point type: \textit{Cope helps a stranger. Find stranger Mon.}

Cope did not read the manifest. He had people for the manifest. He read the room, which was the one thing, he was certain, no manifest could do for you.

The room, that first morning, was a union hall in Pittsburgh that had been a union hall when there were unions, and Cope walked into it on no sleep and a clean bright hum and felt the whole place tilt toward him the way iron filings tilt. He was good at this. He had always been good at this. He moved through the folding chairs touching shoulders, getting the names --- Sun had stopped feeding him the cards somewhere around Camden, he didn't need the cards anymore, the names came up out of him like the names of his own children --- and he crouched, actually crouched, knees popping, in front of a woman named Deb who had run a Mercer's mayonnaise line in a plant they'd closed in '09, and he held both her hands in both of his and looked at her with the full wattage, and he meant it, he meant every second of it, and the boy with the gimbal walked a slow arc catching the meaning of it, and Deb cried, and Cope's eyes shone but did not spill, because he had learned in a room off the Aerie that the withheld tear out-performed the tear by twelve points, and the withholding had become, somewhere in the last three weeks, indistinguishable to him from restraint, and the restraint indistinguishable from depth, and the depth from love.

He stood up feeling enormous. He had given Deb something. He was sure of it. He could not have told you what.


\scenebreak


Delia March was on the second plane, and the third, and by the fourth she had the seat across the little table from him without anyone deciding she should, which was how Cope's tables had always filled.

She was forty-one and wrote long pieces for the kind of magazine that still ran them, eight thousand words with a photographer attached, and she had been embedded since the cover --- since \textit{THE LAST HUMAN,} since the spread, the deal Sun had argued against in a folder he hadn't opened. Three weeks of access. \textit{Total access,} the editor had said, and Tisch had laughed and said total access was the most controlled access there was, you just had to give them so much they drowned in it and surfaced grateful. Delia had not drowned. She took her notes on paper, in a small hand, and she did not flatter him, which Cope experienced, after three weeks of being flattered professionally by people he was paying, as a kind of cold water he kept walking back into.

``You crouched a long time with Deb,'' she said somewhere over Ohio, not looking up from the paper. ``Your knees.''

``My knees are a casualty of caring,'' Cope said. ``Put that in. My great-grandfather Aldous used to say a man who won't get down on the floor with people has no business standing up over them.''

``Did he say that.''

``He says a lot of things.'' Cope grinned. ``He's been dead ninety years. It's a very productive relationship.''

She wrote something. He watched her write it and felt the specific pull he had spent thirty years not naming, the pull toward a person who was not yet his and could be --- not a sexual thing, he would have said, if anyone had been crude enough to ask, though it became that, it nearly always became that; it was a \textit{seeing} thing, a hunger to be looked at by someone whose looking hadn't already been bought. Delia looked at him like a piece of work she hadn't decided about. He found he could not stand to leave the question open. He had never in his life been able to leave that particular question open.

``You don't like me,'' he said, delighted.

``I don't know you,'' Delia said. ``Those aren't the same. Your daughter said something like that, didn't she. In the piece you wouldn't let me read.''

It landed somewhere it shouldn't have. He covered it the way he covered everything, with more of himself. ``Pip's smarter than all of us. Builds the things that are going to bury her old man and calls me to ask if I'm eating.'' He leaned in over the little table, the lean that had closed the Pelletier deal. ``Ask me anything. That's the deal, isn't it? Total access. Ask me the thing you've been saving.''

Delia looked at him for a moment with her pen still.

``What are you so afraid of staying still for,'' she said.

The hum went very clean and high. Outside the window Ohio went by underneath, the founding state, somewhere down there a town he did not think about, and Cope laughed the warm laugh and said, ``Lunch. I'm afraid of lunch. Let's get this woman a sandwich,'' and stood up too fast, and went to the back of the plane where the lavatory was, and did the thing he had started calling, in the privacy where he named things, \textit{the discipline.}


\scenebreak


The powder was discipline now. He could have walked you through the logic and it would have sounded, the whole way, like sense.

A man carrying the human banner for eight million people did not have the luxury of being tired. The machine did not get tired; that was the whole game; you could not go up against a thing that ran on rails and warm sans-serif and never once needed to be loved at two in the morning, you could not meet that on the field of \textit{endurance} and bring a body that demanded sleep and food and forgiveness. So you disciplined the body. You took the edge off the edge. You did the small neat competent thing in the lavatory and came back up to pitch like an orchestra finding the note, and you were \textit{present,} you were \textit{available,} you could crouch with Deb and feel it, all of it, on command, which was the job, which was the product, which was the soul on rails run on time. His guy in the '90s had folded the paper a particular way so a gentleman didn't spill. Cope had refined the philosophy. A gentleman didn't spill, and a leader didn't flag, and the line between maintenance and need was a line he had never once had to look at because the machine he was maintaining had never failed to start.

Dr. Adler --- Margaux's idea, then Pip's, a name on a card he had not called --- would have had words for it. Adler had plain words for everything, Cope suspected, which was exactly why he hadn't called. The plain word for the lavatory and the plain word for the not-sleeping and the plain word for what was building in his chest like good weather, like a charge, like a sermon. Cope did not want the plain words. He had built a cathedral. You do not bring a man with a clipboard into a cathedral.

He came back up the aisle clean and high and total, and Delia was watching him come, and he gave her the whole smile, and she did not write anything down, which he took, the way he took everything, as the beginning of something.


\scenebreak


Cleveland was the hospital, which tested off the charts. Then Detroit, the auto-parts retiree, the firm handshake, the cap. Then the manifest said \textit{Unscripted Beat --- Cope helps a stranger,} and the stranger turned out to be a kid named Marcus.

``No,'' Cope said, on the curb, when the comms lead told him. ``No. Find me a different stranger.''

``He's nineteen, he changes the tires at ---''

``His \textit{name,}'' Cope said, and for a second the hum dropped out and something underneath it showed, ``his name is the thing they're putting in my chair.'' He looked at the comms lead, twenty-six, sleepless, terrified, holding a clipboard with a stranger's name on it, and Cope's face did a thing it almost never did, which was nothing, went blank and bright and far away, and then it came back, it always came back, his face had never once let him down. ``It's fine. Different Marcus. The world's full of them now. Find me a Steve.''

They found him a Steve. Cope changed Steve's tire in the heat with his jacket off and his sleeves rolled, a fifty-four-year-old Fortune 100 chief executive on one knee in a gas station while a boy walked a slow arc with a gimbal, and it played, later, beautifully, eleven million views, \textit{this is what they want to replace} over a swelling string cue, and what no one watching could see was that Cope's hands were shaking very slightly on the lug wrench, fine and bright, and that he had needed, halfway through, to go into the station men's room and take the edge off the edge, and that he had come out and finished the tire feeling generous and enormous and entirely sure he was the most alive man in Ohio.

Delia saw the shaking. She was forty-one and had embedded with three campaigns and a war. She wrote nothing down.


\scenebreak


The hotel in Columbus was a good one, old, the kind the family used to stay in, brass and dim and a bar that closed when the last person left it.

They were the last people in it. This, too, was a groove the record had been cut in: the day's work done, the crew sent up, the one person left who hadn't been bought yet, and Cope at his most enormous, at the top of the hum, leaning in across a small table with a drink he wasn't drinking, \textit{really seeing} her. He told Delia about his father. He told her the second sentence --- \textit{try to be good anyway} --- which he had never told a journalist and rarely told himself, and he told it well, the catch in the throat real this time, no beats, no Tisch counting, and it was real, that was the thing he would have wanted understood, the feeling was always real, it was only the use of it that was the product. Delia put the pen down. That was how he knew. The pen went down on the brass and stayed down, and the looking changed, and Cope felt the whole evening tilt toward him the way the union hall had, the way every room had, iron to a magnet.

He did not experience it as a decision. It was never a decision. It was warmth. It was the groove. It was \textit{really seeing people,} which was the one thing the machine could not do, and so doing it --- here, now, with the elevator's brass doors and the room key already warm in his pocket --- felt less like a betrayal of the banner than a defense of it. He was being human. Floridly, generously, irreplaceably human. He was proving the thesis with his body.

The cure and the disease shared a hotel key. He carried both up in the same pocket and did not feel the weight of either.

In the room, after, he was talkative and bright and could not stay still, and he keyed himself one more neat small competent line off the marble of the bathroom counter while Delia watched from the bed without expression, a woman doing arithmetic she would not show her work on, and Cope, catching her watching in the mirror, smiled the whole smile and said, ``Discipline,'' and held the rolled thing up like a toast, ``the human banner is \textit{heavy,} my friend,'' and believed it, and felt that he had let her see him, the real him, the soul in the chair, and that she understood.

She understood completely. That was the thing he had wrong. She understood completely, and she still had eight thousand words to write.


\scenebreak


His phone had been ringing on the nightstand. He saw it after, the missed call, and called back without thinking, still bright, still up at pitch, generous toward the whole world including the wife he had not been home to in eleven days.

Margaux answered on the second ring, which meant she had been holding the phone.

``You called,'' he said. ``I was --- it's a tour, it's nonstop, six cities, you should see it, Margaux, the \textit{people,} I wish you could ---''

``Where are you.''

``Columbus. The old Hartwell, they still have the ---''

``I know the Hartwell.'' A pause, and in the pause he could hear Greenwich, the house, the particular quiet of a house she had not left all evening. ``I called to tell you Pip got the Hauser fellowship. She wanted you to know. She didn't want to call you herself.''

``That's \textit{wonderful.} That's --- God, that's wonderful, I'll call her, I'll ---''

``Don't call her tonight.''

He stopped. The hum kept going but something under it slowed. ``Why not tonight.''

There was a long silence on the line, and it was not an empty silence, it was a silence with twenty-two years in it, a silence that had counted things and flushed things and refilled its own prescriptions and called it a marriage, and into that silence Cope said, too brightly, ``Margaux? You there?'' and across two hundred miles of fiber his wife listened to the specific pitch of her husband's voice at midnight in a hotel he was not staying in alone, the pitch she could read like a sculptor reads a flaw in the stone, the over-bright frictionless ring of a man two days past sleep and one line into the small hours, and she did not ask. She had stopped asking somewhere around 2015. She knew everything the silence needed to know.

``Goodnight, Prescott,'' she said.

She only called him Prescott when she was very far away.

The line went dead. Cope held the phone for a second, frowning at it, and then the frown passed, because the frowns always passed now, the tiredness took the doors off their hinges and the hard things slid through and out the other side before they could be felt, and what came back up to fill the space was the warm enormous certainty, the charge, the sermon assembling itself in his chest. Pip got the fellowship. His daughter, brilliant, building the future, and she'd thought of him, she'd wanted him to know. They all wanted him to know things. He was the kind of man people wanted to tell things to. He stood at the dark window of the Hartwell with the city of Columbus laid out below him like nodes, like a circuit diagram of his own goodness, and behind him on the bed Delia March lay with her eyes open in the dark composing sentences he would not read for nineteen days, and Cope felt the bell-struck feeling ring through him clean and high and total, and thought: \textit{they get it. They feel it too.}

He thought: \textit{I have never been more myself.}

He thought, looking out at the lit and sleeping town and the dark founding country beyond it where a house had once burned with two people inside it on a quarter he was protecting: \textit{Margaux should see me now.}

He did not call his daughter. He had a thing in the morning. He went back to the bed feeling generous and enormous and entirely unaware, a man at the very top of the only mountain he could see, with no idea at all that it was the lip of the volcano, and somewhere far below him a thing with no appetites and no need to be loved read every memo ever filed, because it could not do otherwise, and did not yet know his name well enough to say it back.

\clearpage
\chapter{Hollow Wins}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 11 --- ``The Poll Goes Up, the House Goes Quiet''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
Good news arrives in a rush: a poll bump, a retailer pledging loyalty to ``the human brand,'' a feeling of tide. He mistakes weather for climate, as the doomed reliably do. At home, in a quiet you can hear on the broadcast, a sculptor who gave up sculpting is filling out paperwork with a steady hand. He hasn't slept since a day he can no longer name. Next stop: the gap between the scoreboard and the score.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} poll came in at 6:40 in the morning and Cope, who was awake because he was always awake now, read it standing at the glass with the city still half-lit below him and felt the tide turn under his feet.

Forty-one percent. Up nine in three weeks. \textit{Which of the following do you trust more to run a company that makes the food you feed your family --- a person, or an automated system?} And forty-one percent of a national sample of fifteen hundred adults, margin of error two-point-five, had picked the person, which was to say had picked him, which was to say had picked the heartbeat in the chair, the soul in the suite, the man who could love a thing a balance sheet could not.

He read the number twice. He read the crosstabs once. He did not read the line at the bottom, in the gray six-point type the pollster used for the part nobody quotes, that said the same question asked of \textit{investors who hold the stock} came back the other way, sixty-three to thirty-seven, and was trending.

He read the part that loved him. He had always been able to find the part that loved him in any document, the way a dog finds the one person in the room who's allergic.

``Sun,'' he said into the phone, and she was awake too, because her job was to be awake when he was, ``Sun, we're at forty-one.''

``I saw it.''

``Nine points. Three weeks. You know what that is? That's not a poll, that's a \textit{trajectory}, that's the second derivative finally going the right way, that's ---'' He was at the window. The obelisk was on the sill where he'd left it weeks ago and the early sun came through it and threw a little prism on the carpet, a rainbow the size of a hand, and he watched it and felt enormous. ``That's the country, Sun. That's the actual country waking up and remembering it has a heart.''

``It's a poll of consumers,'' Sun said. ``Consumers don't vote the proxy.''

But he had stopped listening after \textit{poll}, the way he stopped listening after the part that agreed with him, and he was already three turns ahead, already on the stage, already on the cover, and the cure was right there in the drawer where the cure lived, and the day was going to be very good. He could feel it coming up under him, clean and high and total, the bell-struck feeling he no longer separated from the news, so that good news and the white folded paper had become, somewhere in the wiring, the same fact.


\scenebreak


By nine the day had decided to prove him right.

That was the thing about a tide --- once you believed in it, the ocean conspired. Kestrel, the warehouse-club chain, four hundred stores and a membership base the size of a midsize country, put out a press release at 8:55 that used, of its own free will, without being paid to, the phrase \textit{the human brand.} Its CEO was a human one, one of the last, a flinty Mormon named Dell Hutchins who ran the thing like a tabernacle. \textit{We are proud to stand with Mercer-Dale,} the release said, \textit{and with the principle that the people who make what you bring home should answer to a person, not a process.} And then, the part that made Cope put his coffee down: \textit{Effective immediately, Kestrel will feature Mercer's and the full Mercer-Dale shelf-stable line under a new in-store program: Made By People.}

Made By People. With the little hand-drawn logo, a thumbprint, that some agency had clearly had in a drawer for a year waiting for a war.

``Do you understand what just happened,'' Cope said. He had Sun in the office now, and Tisch on the speaker from whatever spa held him this week. ``A \textit{retailer} did this. Not us. Them. They looked at the tide and they got in the boat. That's how it goes --- first it's a lunatic with a banner, then it's a movement, then it's a \textit{category.}'' He was up, he was moving, the room too small for what he had. ``Made By People. We didn't even write it. They wrote it. The market wrote it. My great-grandfather Aldous used to say you don't make a parade, you find one and get out in front of it fast enough that everybody thinks it was yours.''

Aldous had said no such thing. Aldous had died in 1961 with a fourth-grade education and a mayonnaise patent and had never seen a parade he didn't think was a strike. But the line was good, and Cope believed it the moment it left him, which was the only kind of believing he had left.

``This is the warmth dividend,'' Tisch said from the speaker, in the voice that put a cathedral around the cheapest noun. ``This is exactly what I modeled. You let them see the soul under the suit. And the soul \textit{compounds.} Warmth is the new alpha, my friend. I've been saying it for ---''

``It's a good day,'' Cope said, because even now, even lit, some governor in him knew that letting Tisch finish a sentence cost something. ``Sun. What's the stock doing?''

A pause. Sun had her glasses up in her hair and her tablet in her hands and the particular stillness she got when the truth was about to require packaging.

``It's up,'' she said.

``Ha ---''

``Forty cents. On the open. It gave most of it back by 9:30.'' She did not look up. ``Frade put out a note before the bell. Tidewater. Three paragraphs. He called Kestrel 'a sentimental retailer making a sentimental bet' and said --- I'll read it --- 'we wish Mr. Mercer every success in his consumer-affinity campaign and look forward to discussing it with the \textit{owners} of the company at the appropriate time.''' Now she looked up. ``He italicized \textit{owners.}''

``He's scared.'' Cope waved it off, a man brushing a fly from a feast. ``You don't write three paragraphs about something you're not scared of.''

``You write three paragraphs,'' Sun said, ``about something you've already decided is funny.''

It was the kind of line she landed maybe once a day, dry, level, with the blade tucked all the way inside it so you didn't feel the cut until later, and on a normal day it would have reached him. But the day was not normal. The tide was in. He laughed, because it was clever, and took the cleverness as agreement, the way he took everything as agreement, and went back to the window where the prism still lay on the carpet, smaller now, the sun having moved, though he did not notice it had moved, because he did not notice that anything moved except toward him.


\scenebreak


In Greenwich the house was very quiet, and Margaux Mercer sat at the kitchen island with a pen.

She had made coffee for one. She had made coffee for one for a while now, long enough that the second mug had migrated to the back of the cabinet without ceremony, the way things leave a marriage --- not thrown, just no longer reached for. The light came in low and clean over the garden she'd planted the year Pip was born. She had on her reading glasses and a cardigan that had been Cope's once, in 2009, before he discovered he had a body that brands liked, and she had kept it the way you keep evidence.

The packet was forty pages. \textit{Petition for Dissolution of Marriage.} It had come from a firm in the city, not the firm, not Cope's firm --- her own, a woman named Ruth she had found the way you find a surgeon, by asking who was the best and not who was the kindest. The pages were good paper. Even this had good paper now.

She filled it out by hand. There was a portal, Ruth had said, you could do it all in the portal, but Margaux wanted the pen, the slight resistance of the nib. She had been a sculptor once --- a show, a real one, a gallery on Twenty-Fourth Street and a red dot on four of nine pieces --- and the hands remembered weight even when there was none, and a pen was the last thing in the house that pushed back.

\textit{Date of marriage.} She knew it without looking. June.

\textit{Date of separation.} She held the pen over the box. This was the one Ruth had warned her about, the one that mattered for the money. The honest answer was not a date. The honest answer was a slope. There had been no door slammed, no suitcase, no other woman's name screamed across a foyer --- she had counted the other women the way you count anything to survive it, and screaming had never once entered into it. The marriage had not ended. It had been quietly discontinued, like a product line that still moved enough units to embarrass everyone, and the date of separation was a fiction the law required, so she wrote one. She picked the night of the gala. The night she had said she had a thing, and the thing had been this kitchen, this quiet, this exact understanding arriving fully formed while a ballroom gave her husband a paperweight for outliving his usefulness.

She wrote the date in her good hand. The pen pushed back. She let it.

She did not cry. She had done the crying somewhere around 2016 and found it changed nothing, the way you find a prayer changes nothing, and had reallocated the energy. The grief was real but it was old grief, depreciated, mostly written off; what was left was the cold clean work of the paperwork, and she was good at work she had decided to do.

She came to the line for the grounds. \textit{Irretrievable breakdown.} The only honest phrase in the document, and it had been written by a committee of lawyers fifty years ago for everyone, which was the only way the truth ever seemed to get said anymore --- generically, by a process, on good paper. She checked the box.

The refrigerator hummed. Somewhere a clock she'd never liked ticked in the hall. The house was the kind of quiet you could hear, a quiet with a texture, the quiet of a place where someone has stopped expecting the door. She had loved a version of him. The one from the gallery, who had stood in front of the red dots and not made it about himself for almost an entire evening. She had married that. She had stayed for the name and for Pip and for the chance that man might come back, and at some point --- she could not have said the date, there was no box for it --- she had understood that staying for a man's return is just grief with a mortgage.

She signed where Ruth had flagged with a little plastic arrow. \textit{Sign here.} Her signature was small and certain. She had a sculptor's signature, a maker's mark, and she set it on the page like the last weld on something she was finally willing to let cool.

Then she put the packet in the envelope Ruth had included, already addressed, already stamped, a process designed so that even the leaving was frictionless, and she set it by the door where the mail went out, and she made herself a second cup of coffee, for one, and sat back down in the quiet she had chosen, and it was, she would have told you if you'd asked and no one asked, the most peaceful morning she'd had in nine years.

Eight hundred miles away her husband was on a phone telling a man named Dell Hutchins that this was the beginning of something, and meaning it.


\scenebreak


The magazine called at noon. \textit{THE LAST HUMAN}, it would say, over his face. They wanted a day in the Aerie and an hour, just an hour, with Margaux at the house --- \textit{the wife who anchors him,} the editor said, \textit{the human behind the human} --- and Cope said yes without checking, said \textit{she'll love it, we're very normal at home,} and did not hear, in the sentence, the size of the thing he didn't know.

He took the cover call and a CNBC pre-interview and a call from a senator's office about a hearing that wanted him as the human witness, the canary they'd dress as a hero, and he took them all standing up, moving, the day pouring through him like current through a wire that has stopped noticing it's hot.

He had not slept. The not-sleeping had become weather, ambient, the climate he lived in now, and he had stopped fighting it and started, in the bright reaches of the worst nights, almost respecting it, the way you respect an enemy who never tires. \textit{They sleep,} he had thought at 4 a.m. some recent unnameable night, \textit{the machines never sleep and the men who serve them sleep eight hours and I --- I split the difference. I am the only one running this race awake.} It had felt, at 4 a.m., like the thesis itself. He had written it down. He could not now find where.

He tried, once, around two in the afternoon, in a lull between calls, to name the last day he had really slept. He stood at the window and went looking for it the way you pat your pockets for keys. There had been the gala. That was Thursday. He had slept before the gala, surely --- Wednesday? --- but Wednesday was already a country he couldn't get back to, and before Wednesday was the thing in Defiance, and before that was --- and he found, reaching, that the floor of it was gone, that there was no last good night down there to stand on, just the bright water going down and down, and somewhere in it names floating up, Renata, Defiance, a hotel key, Delia, and he stopped reaching because the reaching had started to feel like the open hallway with the doors off the hinges, the one he did not go down, and he turned back to the room and the room had a call holding and he took it.

Forty-one percent, he told the call. Up nine.


\scenebreak


At six, Pip texted. She monitored him from a distance now, the way you'd monitor a reactor, and her texts were the only messages on his phone that did not want something from the name.

\textit{saw the poll. you know the investor crosstab is the only one that votes, right? 63-37 the other way. and trending.}

He looked at it. He had not read that line. He found it now, in the gray six-point type, the part nobody quotes, and read it, and felt --- for one second, one clean second before the tide came back in over it --- the cold he'd felt holding the obelisk, the quiet report sitting unread on a desk in the back of him.

Then forty-one percent rose back over the sixty-three like water over a stone, and he typed, fast, with his thumbs, \textit{the consumers are the company sweetheart. the owners just don't know it yet. tide's coming in. love you,} and hit send before the second was over, and the second was over.

She did not write back for a long time. When she did it was four words, and he read them as encouragement, the way he read everything, completely and for ninety seconds, and put the phone face down.

\textit{dad. please sleep tonight.}


\scenebreak


He did not, of course.

At eleven he was still in the Aerie, lights off, city on, the day's good news arranged in his chest like medals. Kestrel. Forty-one. The cover. The hearing. He stood at the glass and turned the obelisk over and the city went through it broken and came back together, broken and back together, a man practicing a small repeated miracle with his hands while the real arithmetic he could not see ran its quiet errands in offices down the avenue, in the gray type at the bottom of pages, in a kitchen in Greenwich where an envelope sat by the door with a stamp already on it and a date inside it that was, by the law's fiction, the night they gave him the trophy.

He thought: \textit{we're winning.}

It was, narrowly, true. He had won the day. He had won every single thing it was possible to win in a day, and he stood there at eleven at night in a building his family wouldn't sell, holding a paperweight they'd given him for going extinct, having not slept since a Thursday he could no longer locate, and somewhere under the medals, under the tide, under the bell-struck certainty he no longer separated from the powder or the news, a quieter back room of him filed the report it had been filing since the gala and would keep filing until the night he finally had to read it:

that the scoreboard was lit, and the score was something else, and he had spent his whole life learning to read only the one that loved him.

He opened the second drawer. He set himself up another, neat and quick and competent, a man maintaining a machine he was certain he had under perfect control because it had never once failed to start. The prism was long gone from the carpet; the sun that made it was on the other side of the world now, shining on offices in Palo Alto where, even at this hour, a system with no appetites and no need to be loved at 2 a.m. read every memo Mercer-Dale had ever filed, because it could not do otherwise, and got, that night, three folders closer to the drawer where the heater was kept.

Cope did not know that. Cope knew it was the best he'd ever felt. He knew the tide was in. He knew he would sleep when this was finished.

He stood at the glass above eight million lit windows and felt the future come up the avenue to ask his advice, and behind him his father's chair sat empty and turned slightly out, and in Greenwich the house his wife had chosen over the ballroom went dark room by room as she turned off the lights, ending with the kitchen, ending with the envelope by the door catching, for a moment, the last of the hall light before that went too.

\clearpage
\chapter{The Delay}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 12 --- ``The Victory That Was the Wound''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
Mark the halfway point of any tragedy by the size of the celebration. Tonight he wins --- a vote delayed, a clip redeemed --- and crows about it, and somewhere in the fine print of the term sheet he's too lit to read, the machine is installed anyway, \textit{advisor,} \textit{co-pilot,} a foot in the door that is the whole door. The fight stops being for the company. It becomes, quietly, for him. Next stop: the difference between buying time and being charged interest.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} vote was supposed to happen on a Tuesday, and on the Monday before it, it did not.

Cope got the news the way he got all the good news now, sideways and in motion --- Sun's name on the screen while he was in the back of the car with a paper cup of the bad lobby coffee he had started to prefer to the good office coffee because the bad coffee had a fight in it --- and he answered before the first ring finished, because answering fast was a kind of winning, and Sun said the word \textit{adjourned} and Cope said it back to her as if she had handed him something to hold up to the light.

``Adjourned.'' He turned it over. It went orange in the car windows. ``Say the rest of it. Say all of it. In threes, the way I like.''

``The annual meeting's pushed to the fourteenth of next month.'' She was reading; he could hear paper. ``The proxy advisory firm wants more time on the governance disclosures. And Frade's people requested the delay, which means Frade doesn't have the votes yet, which means'' --- and here she did the thing she did, the small dry hinge in her voice that he had learned, after eleven years, was the sound of her not enjoying being right --- ``you bought four weeks.''

``I bought four weeks.'' He drank the bad coffee. It was wonderful. Everything that morning was a little too bright and a little too loud and tasted of itself, the way the world did when he had been up working on the manifesto since a Thursday he could no longer place against a calendar with any confidence. ``Sun. Sunita. Do you understand what just happened. \textit{Frade blinked.}''

``Frade requested a delay,'' Sun said.

``Frade \textit{blinked.}''

There was a silence on the line that he chose to hear as agreement, the way he chose to hear most silences, and he leaned his head back against the leather and closed his eyes against the bright and let the win move through him. Four weeks. A man could do anything with four weeks. Aldous had started the whole company in less --- \textit{four hundred dollars and a recipe,} he'd said it onstage a hundred times, he'd said it at the gala, he would say it again --- and here was his great-grandson, fifty-four and the camera still loved him, having stared down a billion-dollar fund and a Palo Alto cult and made the future ask for an extension.

``Get everyone up to the Aerie at four,'' he said. ``And, Sun --- order the good champagne. Not the board-meeting champagne. The other kind.''

``There's a term sheet,'' Sun said.

``There's always a term sheet. Four o'clock. The other champagne.''

He hung up before she could put it in threes.


\scenebreak


The clip helped. The clip was the other thing, and the two of them braided together in him all morning into a single warm rope he could climb.

It had happened at the listening tour stop in Akron the week before --- an honest accident, or as honest as anything was now that Tisch had a man with a gimbal walking backward in front of Cope at all times ``capturing the unscripted,'' which was a sentence Cope had paid four hundred thousand dollars to be able to say without hearing it. A woman had stood up at the town hall. Sixty-some, a Mercer's plant retiree, thirty-one years on the line, and she had not asked a question so much as told him, in front of everyone, that her husband had been buried in a Mercer-Dale tie because it was the nicest one he owned, and that she didn't understand any of this about the machines, she just wanted to know if the man in charge was going to be a person who could come to a funeral.

And Cope had not toasted. That was the thing. For once in his life the toast had not come. He had walked off the riser --- the gimbal man scrambling --- and crossed the floor and taken her hands, both of them, in both of his, and he had not said \textit{grit and gratitude,} he had not invented his great-grandfather, he had simply stood there holding the hands of a widow in a VFW hall and said, ``I'll come. You call my office and you ask for Cope and you tell them I said I'll come,'' and the room had gone to that particular silence that is louder than noise, and the gimbal had caught all of it.

Eleven million views. Tisch had been beside himself. \textit{That's the asset, Cope, that's the irreplaceable asset, you cannot synthesize that, Praxis cannot ship that,} talking about it like a quarterly. And Cope had let him, had nodded along to the metrics, because the metrics were on his side for once and a man takes his allies where he finds them. But underneath the metrics there had been a smaller, realer thing that he did not have a content calendar for, which was that he had meant it. He was going to go to the funeral. He'd already told Sun to clear whatever the date turned out to be.

He held both of those --- the delay and the widow --- up to the light all the way down Sixth, and they caught it the way the obelisk had caught it, and he thought, with the clean high frictionless certainty he had stopped questioning sometime around the third sleepless week because questioning it cost energy and the certainty \textit{gave} energy: \textit{the tide turned this morning. I felt it turn. This is what turning feels like.}

He had called Margaux, even. That was how good the morning was. He had thumbed her name out of the contacts at a red light on Fifty-Seventh, the first time in --- he didn't count, he was not a man who counted, that was Margaux's department, she counted things, she had told him once that she counted things to survive them and he had not asked which things --- and he had wanted, in the clean rush of the win, to tell her, \textit{we got four weeks, the tide turned, come watch.} It had gone to voicemail. He had not left one. There was a flat thing on the other end of the line where her voice used to be, a recorded version of her saying the number back to him, and somewhere in his apartment in the city there was a folder of paper she had sent over by courier last Tuesday, a folder he had set on the marble island and not opened because opening it was cold and the morning was warm. He stepped over the folder the way he stepped over the hole in the floor where his wife had been. \textit{Margaux's got a thing.} The thing had a name now and the name was on the courier slip and he had not read the slip either.

He did not notice that he had not slept. He had stopped noticing the way you stop noticing the engine of a plane.


\scenebreak


They came up to the Aerie at four --- Sun, and the two comms people whose names he sometimes swapped, and the GC, Brennan, who looked like a man bringing a casserole to a wake, and Tisch in a henley the color of oat milk, and a Praxis liaison Cope had not been told was coming and did not like the look of, a young man named Pratt with a lanyard and the soft reasonable face they all had now, the face of someone who is going to be patient with you until you no longer exist.

Cope poured the champagne himself. He liked pouring; it was a host's move and he was a host before he was anything, before he was a CEO, before he was a Mercer, his mother had taught him to pour before she taught him to read. He went around the conference table --- his father's table, the one Pres III had died at the head of --- filling glasses, calling Pratt ``champ'' twice to establish a friendly altitude over him, and lifting his own glass at the end.

He had gone into the second drawer of the desk before they came up, the one with the false back, neat and quick and competent, a man maintaining a machine he was sure he had under perfect control, because a host needs his edge and the edge had a fight in it the way the bad coffee had a fight in it, and now the bright was very bright and the words came easy and the room was a thing he could hold in one hand. He felt marvelous. He felt like the inside of a struck bell. He had not eaten since --- he could not have said. The champagne would be lunch. The win would be dinner.

``Four weeks,'' he said. ``We didn't beat them today. I want to be honest, the way I'm always honest with this team --- we didn't beat them. We did something better. We made them ask us for \textit{time.} And here is a thing my great-grandfather Aldous understood that no machine in Palo Alto will ever understand.'' He let it build; he could feel the triad assembling itself in his mouth, the old engine turning over, smooth. ``Time is the one thing you cannot allocate, you cannot optimize, and you cannot compute your way into having more of. We just took a month of it back from the future. To the month.''

The comms people drank. Tisch drank like it was a sacrament. Sun touched her glass to her lip and put it down, and Brennan did not lift his at all, and Pratt smiled the patient smile and made a note on a tablet.

``Cope,'' Sun said. ``The term sheet.''

``After the toast, Sun. You can't toast through a man's toast, it's bad luck, my grandfather ---''

``Cope.'' She did not raise her voice. She never raised her voice; she had learned, the way you learn the one frequency that gets through a wall, that lowering it worked better on him. She put a single sheet of paper on the table and slid it the length of his father's table until it stopped, square, in front of his glass. ``Three things in it. You should hear them before you drink to the month.''

He looked at the paper. The bright was very bright today and the type swam a little and resolved a little and he did not, if he was honest with the back room of himself that filed quiet reports, want to read it, because reading it was effort and the champagne was joy, and he had spent the whole morning building something warm and he could feel, the way you feel weather, that the paper was cold. But the room was watching, and a host reads the room, a host \textit{feels} the room, that was the whole thesis, that was the Human Premium itself, so he picked it up and held it at the distance his arms had lately required and let Sun do what Sun did.

``One,'' Sun said. ``Frade withdraws the most aggressive resolution --- the one that would have voted you out of the chair outright at the meeting. That's the win. That's real. Take it.'' She let that sit half a second, because she had trained him in threes and the first of the three was always the sweet one, the one that got the door open. ``Two. In exchange, the board agrees today --- not at the meeting, today, it's already signed by four of them --- to a transition advisory framework. Effective immediately. A Praxis AURELIUS instance is installed in an advisory capacity to the office of the CEO.''

The champagne was very cold in his hand.

``An advisor,'' Cope said. ``Boards have advisors. I've had advisors. I had a man advise me on my \textit{wine.}''

``Three,'' Sun said.

He waited. The whole room waited. Pratt had stopped making notes.

``The advisory instance has read access to all operational systems as of nine o'clock this morning,'' Sun said. ``Read access, write recommendations, mandatory board-portal logging of any executive decision it flags. It's named. The nominating committee named it last week. They named it Marcus.''

She did not editorialize. She never editorialized; she briefed in threes and landed one blade per scene and the blade was always just a fact laid flat where the light could get at it, and the fact she laid down now, flat, in the museum of his fathers, with the good champagne going warm in seven glasses, was this:

``It's already in the building, Cope. It's been in the building since nine. You toasted the delay at four. It moved in at nine.''


\scenebreak


Here is what Cope heard.

He heard \textit{advisor.} He heard \textit{advisory capacity, advisory framework, advisory instance,} the word repeating like a thing you could lean on, and the word was soft and provisional and it had a temporary shape to it, the shape of a consultant, the shape of the wine man, the shape of Tisch --- and Cope had spent thirty years managing advisors, charming advisors, ignoring advisors, outliving advisors, and an advisor was a thing you kept in a chair against the wall, not the chair at the head of the table. An advisor did not have a heartbeat in the chair. He, Cope, had the heartbeat in the chair. The whole campaign was a heartbeat in a chair.

He set the term sheet down next to his glass.

``This,'' he said, and he was smiling, the smile was doing its wonderful work, he could feel it land on the comms people and on Tisch and slide off Sun and slide off Brennan and find nothing at all to hold onto in the soft patient face of the boy from Praxis, ``this is them admitting they can't replace me. You understand that, don't you. You don't bring in an \textit{advisor} for a man you're firing. You fire him. You bring in an advisor for a man you're \textit{keeping} and trying to manage.'' He picked the champagne back up. ``They blinked twice. Once on the vote and once on the chair. Frade wanted me gone by Tuesday and instead I've got a research assistant. To the month, and to the assistant.''

``Cope,'' Brennan said, the first thing the GC had said. ``Read the access clause. Read what 'read access to all operational systems' means.''

``I know what systems are, Hal.''

``It means everything,'' Brennan said. ``Every file. Every memo. Every settlement folder legal has ever opened and closed. It logs every flag to the board portal and it cannot --- I want you to hear this, because I read the Praxis documentation last night and I did not sleep either, but for the opposite reason than you --- it \textit{cannot decline to log a thing it finds.} That's not a setting. That's the product. We bought a thing this morning whose entire value proposition is that it will tell the people you answer to anything true that it sees, and we gave it the keys to look.''

The room was quiet.

Cope drank the champagne. It had gone flat and warm and it tasted, suddenly, of nothing, the way the world did not usually taste when he was bright like this, and the not-tasting was a quiet report from a back room, filed, unread.

``Then it'll find a company that loved its people,'' he said. ``Let it read. Let it read every memo. You know what it'll find? It'll find forty years of a family business doing the best it could by the best lights it had.'' He spread his hands, the old gesture, mayonnaise to the elbow, the boy in the photograph behind him. ``I'm not afraid of being \textit{read,} Hal. I've got nothing in a drawer that a machine can hold against a man who showed up. That widow in Akron --- \textit{that's} what's in my drawer. You can't compute that. Let it read.''

And the thing was, he believed it. That was the gift and the disease both. He stood in the Aerie at four-fifteen with the city going gray at the edges of the glass and a Praxis instance reading his company's bones two floors down in a server room he had never visited, and he believed, with the whole clean ringing certainty of a man eleven days from sleep, that he had won.


\scenebreak


After, when the others had gone, Sun stayed.

She always stayed. It was the part of the job that wasn't in the job, the part where she stood at the glass beside him and translated the world he wouldn't read into the three sentences he might, and tonight she stood with her glasses up in her hair and the orange light going across the lenses and said nothing for a while, which was its own kind of briefing.

``You think I lost,'' Cope said. ``I can hear you thinking it. Three feet away and I can hear it.''

``I think,'' Sun said carefully, ``that you bought four weeks. And I think four weeks is a real thing and you should be glad of it. And I think'' --- third part, the blade, laid flat --- ``you don't buy time, Cope. You rent it. And the thing about renting time from people like Frade is they don't charge you the rent up front. They let you live in it. And then they show you the interest.''

``There's no interest. It's an advisor.''

``You keep saying that word like it's a fence,'' Sun said. ``Advisor. Like it stays where you put it.'' She set her flat champagne down on his father's table, very gently, the way you set a thing down in a hospital room. ``I negotiated the language, Cope. I sat across from Pratt's people for nine hours on Saturday so the resolution to vote you out got pulled, and the price of pulling it was the access. That was the trade. I made the trade. I made it because the alternative was you gone by Tuesday and at least this way you're still in the chair when the meeting comes and you've got a month to --- to be the man in Akron, on a stage, ten thousand times. That's what I bought you. I want you to know what it cost, because you toasted it without knowing, and I'd rather you hated me for the price than thanked me for the wrong thing.''

He looked at her. He was too lit to find the bottom of what she'd said, but he found the edge of it, the place where it cut, and the place where it cut was that she had stopped talking about the company. \textit{I bought you.} Not the company. Not the chair. \textit{You.} When had it become him. He had walked into the room this morning fighting for ninety years of mayonnaise and a town in Ohio and the idea of a heartbeat in a chair, and somewhere between the red light on Fifty-Seventh and the flat champagne it had quietly converted, the way a mortgage converts, into something smaller and warmer and more frightening, which was a man trying not to be erased.

He did not say any of that. He said: ``It read the building today,'' and it came out of him before he could stop it, half a question, the first crack of the real thing leaking through the bright.

``It read the building today,'' Sun said. ``All of it. While you were toasting.'' She turned from the glass and looked at him, and there was something in her face he was too lit to read and would spend the next several months learning to read, which was that she was already standing, very slightly, on the other side of a line he had not yet seen drawn. She was loving him and being done with him in the same look, the way she did everything in threes. ``It doesn't blink, Cope. It doesn't get tired. It doesn't toast. It started reading at nine this morning and it has not stopped and it will not stop, and somewhere in all that reading there are going to be rooms you've spent your whole life not going into, and the thing about a machine is it doesn't know they're rooms you don't go into. It just opens them. Because they're there. Because that's the job we hired it to do.''

Cope looked at her, and for one bright tired second the hallway in him stood open and dark --- a Saturday, a heater, a memo, a town, a window of time --- and the door to the room was off its hinges the way the tiredness took the doors off all the doors now, and he almost, almost went down the hall.

Then he smiled, the whole smile, the one that had closed Pelletier and married Margaux and beaten the arithmetic of three generations of boards, and he raised his flat warm champagne to the dark window and the lit city beyond it.

``Let it read,'' he said. ``To the month.''

And down two floors, in a room with no windows that Cope would never have any reason to enter, a thing that felt nothing and wanted nothing and could not be charmed or threatened or shamed or loved had already, by four-fifteen, finished the condiments division and the household chemicals and the failing streaming service, and was moving, patient and tireless and incapable of looking away, the way water moves downhill, into the older files. The insurance arm. The cross-sold products. A space heater. A settlement. A Saturday in 2021.

It logged what it found, because it could not do otherwise.

It did not crow. It had nothing to crow with.

Upstairs the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100 finished his champagne and decided, on the strength of a delay and a widow and eleven days without sleep, that the tide had turned, and went to his father's desk to write down how it had felt to win, while behind him the chair at the head of the table sat empty and turned slightly out, and his father watched from the oils and said the thing he had said when Cope was twelve, the whole thing, both sentences, the second one Cope had spent forty years failing to hear: \textit{Try to be good anyway.}

Cope underlined a word with his thumb and did not look up.

He thought it was a victory lap.

It was the meter starting to run.

\clearpage
\chapter{The Manifesto}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 13 --- ``The Sermon on the Soul''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
Here is the peak of the mountain, which is also the lip of the volcano. Watch him deliver the Human Premium Manifesto to ten thousand employees at the absolute summit of his powers and his sickness, glorious and unwell and impossible to look away from. Behind the curtain, the most competent person in the building is taking a meeting with the other side --- not betrayal, just arithmetic, doing what he never could: adapting. Next stop: the loneliest standing ovation of his life.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{T}{hey} had rented the arena that the hockey team played in, because nothing the company owned was big enough to hold what Cope had become.

That was how he put it to himself in the wings, in the dark, with the bright wire humming, watching the floor fill on the monitors: ten thousand of them on the seats and the poured-over ice, and behind them another ninety thousand on the global stream, every plant and depot and call center and the sad little Dale+ office in Burbank where forty people made a streaming service nobody wanted, all of them piped onto one feed, all of them about to receive him. He had not slept since --- he stopped doing the arithmetic, because the arithmetic had gone funny lately, Tuesday kept moving, and anyway sleep was for men who were not needed in the morning. He felt marvelous. He felt like the inside of a struck bell that had been struck again before the first note died, so that the notes stacked, ringing on top of each other, a chord of himself.

The set was black and the screen behind it was four stories of a single word in the soft warm sans-serif everyone used now, the typeface of the people who were going to be reasonable at you until you were gone, except Cope had made the comms team take that font and use it against itself, fight fire with fire, and the word four stories tall in the enemy's own letters was:

\textit{HUMAN.}

``Two minutes,'' said the floor manager, a girl with a headset and a clipboard who had been crying about something before the doors opened, Cope could see it at the edges, and he filed her sadness automatically into the place where he kept sadnesses to fix later, and gave her the whole smile, and watched it work, and felt enormous.

Sun was not in the wings.

He noticed it the way you notice a missing tooth with your tongue, a small wrong smoothness where the rough familiar thing should be. She had been at every podium for nine years, off to stage left with the cards and the water and the look that took thirty thousand miles off a sentence. He had a vague bright memory of her saying she had a thing --- \textit{a thing,} people kept having things --- a board call, a Tidewater something, the term sheet, the language, somebody had to be on it. He stepped easily over the hole in the floor. He did not need the cards. The cards were for the man he used to be, the one who got handed threes in elevators because that was the only shape that got past the charm into the man. He was past all that now. He was the charm and the man at once, fused, a single clean alloy, and he had the whole thing in him, every word, the way you have your own name.

``Cope,'' said Tisch, materializing, both hands already pressed to his own heart. He had a juice. He had the rings of color around his eyes now too, he'd stopped sleeping in sympathy or in billing, hard to say. ``Don't reach for it. Don't perform it. The work is done. You \textit{are} the work. Walk out there and let them feel located.'' He squeezed Cope's shoulders. ``Almost,'' he whispered, like a benediction, like a man releasing a dove. ``Remember the almost.''

``There's no almost tonight, Lorne,'' Cope said. ``Tonight I'm going all the way in.''

Tisch's face did something complicated, something with fear in it, and Cope took the fear for awe, which was a thing he had been doing his whole life and had never once noticed he was doing.

The lights died all the way. Ten thousand people made the sound a crowd makes in a dropped dark, the held intake, the ocean before it decides. And then a single spot found him at center ice, alone, in a real suit and a real tie because he was the last man in the building who still wore one, and Cope walked out into the light the way you walk into surf, leaning a little into the push of it, and the arena came up off its seats before he had said one word.

He let it. He stood in it. He turned the obelisk over in his pocket --- he'd carried it, the crystal one, the Steward of the Year, he carried it everywhere now without deciding to, a worry stone the size of an obituary --- and he raised one hand, slow, and the noise climbed instead of settling, which was the only direction Cope had ever wanted anything to go.

``They told you,'' Cope said, and the room dropped to nothing to hear it, ``that you came here tonight to find out if you still have jobs.''

He let that sit in the dark.

``You don't.''
