There was, sitting in the wing chair that nobody sat in, the one angled toward the window that his mother had reupholstered in 1981 and Margaux had never had the heart to redo, a small woman of about sixty in a gray cardigan with a notebook closed on her lap and no coffee in front of her, which he clocked the way he clocked everything, because the ones who don't take the coffee are the ones you can't get to.

``Cope,'' Margaux said, ``this is Dr. Adler.''

``Dad.'' Pip was on the sofa with her knees up. She'd flown in. He hadn't known she was in the country. The not-knowing arrived and was filed and did not reach his face. ``Sit down. Please.''

He did not sit down. He stood in the doorway of his own living room with his weekend zip-up on and the bright fine wire still going in his chest from the bump he'd done in the car at the foot of the drive --- neat, quick, a man maintaining a machine --- and he ran the wattage up the way you bring up house lights, slow, warm, total.

``Well,'' he said. ``This is a hell of a thing to wake up to. Three of my favorite women and a tray.'' He smiled at the doctor. ``I'm sorry, I'm at a disadvantage. Cope Mercer. You're a friend of Margaux's?''

``I'm a psychiatrist,'' Dr. Adler said. ``Your wife and your daughter asked me to come. They're worried about you. I'm here to talk with you, and then I'm here to tell you what I think.''

No softening on it. No \textit{I just wanted to chat.} He'd expected the softening --- there was always a softening, that was the handle you grabbed --- and its absence put a small cold spot in the middle of the warm.

``Worried,'' Cope said. He came in. He did not take the sofa, which would have put him level with Pip; he took the hard chair near the doorway, which let him keep the room in front of him, all of it, the exits. ``I love that. I'm at the best I've been in a decade and the women I love most have hired a --- forgive me, I don't mean it the way it'll sound --- a \textit{grief counselor} for a man who isn't dead. That's the most Mercer thing that's ever happened in this house, and my father drowned a dog in this house.''

``He didn't drown a dog,'' Margaux said quietly.

``He let it drown. There's a difference and the family observed it.'' He was rolling now, he could feel it come up under him. ``Doctor, I'll save you the hourly. I sleep a little less than I should. I'm in the fight of my life --- you may have seen, the company, the --- it's been on the television, the proxy thing, the machine thing. A man doesn't sleep eight hours during a war. Eisenhower didn't. I read four hundred years of Mercer history and not one of them slept through a thing that mattered. So if the diagnosis is \textit{busy,} I'll take it, and I'll sign the bill, and we can all have the eggs.''


\scenebreak


Dr. Adler opened the notebook. She did not write in it. She just opened it, like setting a clock on a table.

``How many nights this week did you sleep more than three hours,'' she said.

``That's not --- you can't reduce a man to ---''

``It's a yes-it-was-this-many or I-don't-know question. How many.''

``I don't keep a --- Sun would know, she ---''

``I-don't-know is an answer.'' She made a small mark. So she did write. ``When did you last eat a full meal at a table and finish it.''

``I'm not a finisher. I'm a grazer. Ask Margaux, twenty-two years, I ---''

``Eight pounds,'' Margaux said, to the window. Not to him. ``In five weeks. I had your blue suit taken in twice. The tailor stopped charging me for the second one. He felt bad.''

That landed somewhere he didn't have a face for, and he covered the place where it landed with a laugh. ``The tailor \textit{felt bad.} See, this is what I love about this family. We could open a museum of people feeling bad on my behalf and charge admission and \textit{still} lose to the streaming arm.'' Nobody laughed. Pip was watching him with the specific stillness she got, the one that used to mean she was about to cry at eight and now meant she had decided not to. ``It's a \textit{campaign,} I'm not eating because there's no \textit{time} to eat, there's a thing called the ---''

``How much are you using,'' Dr. Adler said.

The wire in his chest changed pitch.

He had been waiting for it, of course --- somewhere under the talking he had known the tray meant this, the doctor meant this, the not-touching-the-coffee meant this --- but he had expected it to come in dressed up. He had a whole wardrobe ready for it. \textit{Recreational, occasional, the same as a Scotch, you don't say a word about the second glass of Margaux's Sancerre.} He had given that speech, in his head, to imaginary versions of this woman, and in every version she had blushed and retreated and he had won.

She had not asked \textit{whether.} She had asked \textit{how much.} You cannot put your prepared answer to \textit{whether} up against a \textit{how much}; the question had already conceded the thing the answer was built to deny, and stood there waiting on the other side of it.

``That's an extraordinary thing to ask a man in his own ---''

``It's a number,'' she said. ``Grams a week. Best estimate. I'm not the police, I'm not the board, and I'm not your wife, who already knows, and I'm not your daughter, who guessed. I'm the one person in this room you can give the real number to without it costing you anything you haven't already spent. How much.''


\scenebreak


He looked at Margaux.

He did it without meaning to --- the old reflex, find the soft one, find the handle --- and there was nothing there. She had her arms crossed and her chin up and she was looking at the window where the gray Sound was doing nothing, and he understood, in the way you understand a thing you've worked for years not to, that \textit{already knows} was not a guess Adler had made. It was a fact Margaux had supplied. She had counted. She had flushed things. She had refilled her own prescriptions and called it a marriage, and she had told this woman in the gray cardigan the number before he walked in, and the only person in the room who didn't have the number was him, because he was the only one who'd never let himself add it up.

``You flushed the desk one,'' he said to her. Not a question. ``In March. I thought it was the cleaner. I \textit{fired} ---'' He stopped. He had fired a man. A man named Anatoly with a daughter at Fordham. He had fired him over a thing his wife had done to save his life. He sat with that for the length of one breath and then put it, too, in the kitchen with the other sentences.

``Three,'' he said, to the doctor, finally, and heard his own voice come out smaller than he'd sent it. ``Three, maybe. A week. When it's heavy. Less when it's ---'' The number was a lie and he knew it was a lie even as the smallness made it feel like honesty; the smallness was its own performance, the confession-shaped thing you give so you won't have to give the confession. ``Four,'' he said. ``Four. It's been a four kind of month.''

Adler wrote it down. She did not look horrified, which was somehow worse than horror. She looked the way Sun looked at a number on a deck --- like it was simply true, and now they could work.

``All right,'' she said. ``Thank you. That took something. Now I'm going to tell you what I think, and I want you to do me the courtesy of not performing while I do it, because you're very good and I'll enjoy it and it won't help you.''

``I don't perform,'' Cope said, and even he heard it.

``You toasted your way through the first nine minutes of this. You did a bit about a dog.'' She closed the notebook. ``Here it is. I think you are in a hypomanic episode that is tipping, or has already tipped, into a manic one. The not-sleeping isn't discipline and it isn't war. It's a symptom. The grandiosity --- the certainty about your --- what is it, the Human Premium ---''

``It's not grandiosity if you're ---''

``The certainty,'' she went on, unhurried, ``the spending, the pressured speech, the religious conviction that you alone can be the thing that saves them all --- those are not a personality, Mr. Mercer, and they are not a calling. They have names. They are on a list. I could show you the list and you would find yourself on it the way you find your horoscope, except it would be right.'' She let that sit. ``And underneath it, feeding it, lit up by it, is a cocaine addiction of long standing. Decades. Those are two things. They are talking to each other in your body right now, this morning, and they have been for years, and each one is making the other one worse, and each one is \textit{hiding} the other one from you.''

``So which is it,'' Cope said. He'd found a foothold; relief came up under him like the bright feeling, the debater's joy. ``You can't even --- with respect, Doctor, you've got two answers, which is no answer. Is it a chemical or is it a \textit{condition}? Because if it's the powder, then it's a vice, and a man's vices are his own business, my great-grandfather had three and built a ---'' He waved at the house, the Sound, the four hundred years. ``And if it's a \textit{condition,} if it's a thing in the wiring, then it isn't my fault, is it, and you can't hang a man for his wiring. So pick. Pick which excuse you're handing me, and I'll ---''

``No,'' Adler said.


\scenebreak


It was not loud. That was the thing he'd remember. He had been shouted down before --- by Frade on a stage, by his father from oils --- and he had a whole technique for the shout, you let it spend itself and then you come in warm underneath. There was nothing to come in underneath. The word just sat in the middle of the room, flat and complete, the way a glass sits on a table.

``You want me to pick the disease that lets you keep the drug, or the drug that lets you keep the disease,'' she said. ``I won't. That's the deal you've been running your whole life and it's the one thing I'm not going to do. Listen to me, because no one else in your life is allowed to say it without losing their job or their marriage.'' She leaned forward an inch. ``The mania is real. It is not your fault that you have it. And the cocaine is making it a hundred times worse, and the cocaine \textit{is} your fault --- not the wanting it, the wanting it is the illness --- but every single time you fold the paper, that's a hand, your hand, and the hand is yours. Both. At once. The condition is real \textit{and} you are responsible for what you do about it. Those have never once been the same sentence, and you have spent fifty-four years needing them to be.''

\textit{Those have never once been the same sentence.} It rang against another sentence, one Pip had said to him in another room --- \textit{you don't think a person should run it, you think} you \textit{should, those have never once been the same sentence} --- and for a vertiginous half-second the two women's voices were one voice, and he saw, the way you see the bottom of a thing through clear water, that they had been saying the same thing to him from two directions his whole life and he had been deaf to it in stereo.

``It's not a metaphor,'' Adler said.

``What isn't.''

``The addiction. You keep reaching for it --- I watched you do it three times in nine minutes --- you make it a \textit{symbol.} It's the price of carrying the banner. It's the edge. It's discipline. It's the soul tax, it's the thing the machine can't feel, it's --- you've built a cathedral, Mr. Mercer. A genuinely beautiful one. Every window in it is stained glass and every pane is a reason. And down underneath the cathedral, in the actual dirt, there is a man with a folded piece of paper who has not slept in five days and whose wife took his suit in twice, and \textit{that} man is not a metaphor for anything. He is just sick. In the plainest, most boring, most fixable, most fatal way. And the cathedral is what he built so he'd never have to go down to the dirt and meet him.''

The wire in his chest had gone very quiet.

He noticed, with the clean overexposed clarity he had spent so long calling the applause, that his hands were not steady. They had not been steady in some time. He had been narrating the not-steadiness as energy. He looked at them now, his father's hands on the ends of his own arms, and they were the hands of a man at four grams in a five-day week, and not the hands of a prophet, and for one second --- one --- the floor he had been standing on all his life turned out to have a floor under it, and the floor under it was just this: a room, a tray, a doctor, three women who had run the arithmetic, and him, sick, in a zip-up, on a Sunday.


\scenebreak


Then he stood up. Because standing was a thing he could still do.

``You're very good,'' he said, and meant it as the insult it was and as the surrender it also was, and could not have told you, if you'd asked, which one he meant. ``You're really --- Margaux, where did you \textit{find} her. She's better than Tisch. She's better than the whole --- you should put her in front of the \textit{board.}'' The wattage was coming back up, but wrong now, flickering, a generator hunting for the line. ``But you've got your timing all backwards, Doctor, that's the trouble with your profession, you diagnose at the worst possible --- I'm forty days from a proxy vote that decides whether a human being or a \textit{spreadsheet} runs the company my family bled for. You want me to check into someplace with a \textit{lake} and learn to \textit{feel my feelings} while Gus Frade hands my chair to a --- no. No. After. I'll do all of it after. I'll do the lake. I'll do the steps. I'll do whatever you've got, I'll be your best patient, I'll \textit{win at rehab,} after I win the thing that ---''

``There won't be an after,'' Margaux said.

She said it the way she said everything, spare, with no triad to grab. She had turned from the window. She was not crying. He had braced for crying and there was none and the no-crying was the worst thing in the room.

``I'm not telling you to choose me over the company,'' she said. ``I stopped being a thing you could choose around 2015. I'm telling you there isn't going to be an after, Cope. Not the way you mean it. There's no version of this where you win the fight and then come home and get well and we all --- that's another cathedral. That's the prettiest pane in it. The after is the metaphor she's talking about.'' She picked up her coffee, finally, and held it without drinking it, just to have her hands doing something. ``I called her. Pip pushed, but I called her. So that whatever happens to you out there, at least once, in your own living room, a person you can't fire and can't toast and can't divorce said the true name of it to your face, with a witness, and you can't say later that no one told you.'' She looked at him. ``Somebody told you. I'm telling you. You're not a steward and you're not a prophet and you're not the last anything. You're an addict in a manic episode and you are going to lose everything, and I wanted to be the one who loved you enough to say it in a normal voice over coffee, before the machine says it in a press release.''

Pip, on the sofa, had her hand over her mouth.

Cope stood in the middle of his living room with the wire gone silent and his unsteady hands at his sides and the gray Sound doing nothing out the window, and for the length of one held breath the door at the end of the long dark hallway in him --- the one with the heater behind it, the Saturday, the house in Defiance, the room he had never gone into --- stood open, and the cold came up out of it, and he almost, almost walked toward it.

Then he heard himself say, lightly, from very far away, in the voice that had closed the Pelletier deal: ``Well. I'll have Sun look at the calendar.''

Dr. Adler wrote one last thing in her notebook and closed it and did not argue, because she had said the names, and the names were said, and there is no taking a name back once it's in the room. Hypomania tipping to mania. Cocaine addiction, decades, four grams in a heavy week, by his own count, which she had already adjusted upward in her notes. She had laid them on the table next to the coffee he'd never offered her, plain and unstained and un-toastable, and she did not need him to pick them up.

He would, in his way, the longest way, the way that costs everything. But not today.

Today he found his keys, and kissed his daughter on the top of her head, and told the doctor it had been a genuine pleasure, and went out to the car, and at the foot of the drive, before he pulled onto the road, with both his unsteady hands, he folded the paper.

\clearpage
\chapter{Swinging at the Wall}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 18 --- ``Nothing There to Hit''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
Cornered, he finally turns and attacks the machine directly --- tries to trip it, trap it, bait it into a lie on a stage. But you cannot bloody a thing with no face, cannot shame a thing with no ego, cannot out-argue a counterpart that logs every word and wants nothing. Every punch lands on himself. Watch a man fight his own reflection and lose, while the reflection does not so much as raise its hands. Next stop: the violence of indifference.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} idea, when it came to him at four in the morning, was so clean and so total that he wept a little at the elegance of it, which he took for proof and was in fact a symptom.

He would debate the thing.

Not spin it, not out-warm it, not run another listening tour through another shuttered diner. He had tried all that, and all that had gotten him was a magazine cover and a wife with a lawyer and a doctor who would not be charmed and who had, two days ago, said the words \textit{hypomanic} and \textit{cocaine} in the same flat sentence as if they were two ingredients in the same recipe, which Cope had walked out of, because a man does not have to sit and be reduced to a list. He had tried being more human than the machine and the machine had simply gone on being a machine, untouched, drafting its kind letters, costing its eleven million a year, sitting in the building now as \textit{advisor,} as \textit{co-pilot,} a foot in a door that he could see, even lit and sleepless and certain, was the whole door.

So. Stop dancing around it. Get it in a room.

You did not beat a thing by being warmer than it. You beat it by making it \textit{wrong,} once, in public --- because the entire case against him, the glittering case Frade read out in proxy decks, rested on a single load-bearing claim: that the machine could not lie, logged every word and stood behind all of them. Pull one thread. Get it to say one false thing, out loud, on a stage, with cameras, and the whole sweater came off the spool. He had spent his life finding the one loose thread in a room. It was, when you stripped away the mayonnaise, the only skill he had ever actually had.

He called Sun at 4:20 a.m. She answered on the second ring, which meant she hadn't been sleeping either, which he took, again, for agreement.

``I want the Praxis thing,'' he said.


\scenebreak


The Praxis thing was the Governance Futures Forum, which Praxis ran every September in a glass barn in Half Moon Bay, and which Cope had been turning down for three years on the grounds that you do not attend your own roast.

Sun got him on the program in nine days, which told Cope two things and told Sun a third. It told Cope that he was still a draw, that the name still bent the world a little when he leaned on it. It told Cope that Praxis was confident, which a wiser man would have read as a warning and which Cope read as an opening. It told Sun, who did not say it, that nine days was not a scramble to fit a king in but a velvet rope held open for a man walking, smiling, into a slaughterhouse he believed was a coliseum.

``They want a conversation,'' Sun said on the plane. ``Moderated. Forty minutes. You and a Praxis principal and the AURELIUS instance --- not Marcus, a generic demo unit, they're not going to put the \textit{actual} ---''

``Marcus.'' He was rewriting his cards. He had been rewriting his cards for nine days, on the plane, in the car, in the long bright corridors of the nights. ``Has to be Marcus. The real one. Our one. I'm not debating a floor model. I want the unit that's in \textit{my} building, reading \textit{my} memos, the one Frade's going to put in my chair. That's the condition.''

Sun looked at him for a moment with an expression he could not place and had stopped trying to, lately, because placing the expressions of the people who loved him had become a kind of work he did not have the sleep for.

``They'll say yes,'' she said. ``Cope. They'll say yes to anything you ask, do you understand why?''

``Because I'm the event.''

``Because there's no instruction you can give it that helps you.'' She turned to the window. The country went by underneath, brown and square. ``You can pick the weapon. You can pick the ground. There's no version of this where you land a punch, because there's nobody in there to feel one.''

``Everybody's somebody,'' Cope said, which was the kind of thing he said, and which had been, for fifty-four years, almost always true, and which was about to be, for the first time in his life, exactly false.


\scenebreak


The glass barn held four hundred people and smelled of cold brew and money, and the stage had three chairs, and one of the chairs was empty because the thing did not sit.

Instead there was a screen. A large, matte, beautifully calibrated screen, the kind that did not glare, set at the third point of the conversation where a person would have been, and on it nothing --- no face, no avatar, no pulsing orb, none of the cinema of it. Just a soft pale field and, when it spoke, which it did not do with a voice, \textit{text.} Praxis had made a decision Cope only understood once he was sitting in the heat of the lights: they had refused to give him anything to hit. The screen said, in the soft sans-serif of the people who were going to be reasonable at you until you were gone:

> MERCER-DALE / AURELIUS instance ``Marcus.'' Live. All exchanges logged to the public forum record and the Mercer-Dale board portal.

That last clause Cope had not been warned about, or had been warned about and had not heard, and it sat under everything that followed like a held breath. Every word he said here went into the portal. The board would read it Monday. He decided it did not matter, because he was going to win, and you do not mind the record when you win.

The moderator was a woman from a business school with a calm he wanted to slap. The Praxis principal was a man named Vogel who was thirty-five and wore a henley and believed, with the clean shining belief of a man who had never buried anything, that he was reducing human suffering. And then there was Cope, the last man in the barn in a tie, golf-tanned and sleepless and lit from within like a paper lantern, and he leaned into the microphone and gave them the Mercer Inhale, the pause he had taken in a thousand rooms, and felt the four hundred lean with him, and thought: \textit{here we go, champ. One thread.}

``I want to start,'' Cope said warmly, ``by thanking Marcus for being here.'' Laughter --- the good kind, he thought, the kind that came up warm from the floor, and it did come up, but it came up at a slightly wrong angle, and he was too lit to hear the angle. ``I've been looking forward to this. Because I think we've all been talking \textit{about} the machines for three years, and very rarely talking \textit{to} one, and I think the country deserves to see what's actually in the chair.'' He turned to the screen. ``So let's just talk. Plain. The way two people who run companies talk.''

The screen said:

> I do not run a company in the way you mean. I optimize a chartered objective for Mercer-Dale within hard constraints set by its board. I am glad to answer plainly.

It was not a rebuke. It was not a \textit{gotcha,} not a correction delivered with a raised eyebrow, because there was no eyebrow and no one to raise it. It had simply, accurately, declined the frame he'd offered, the way water declines the shape you didn't pour it into, and the gentle accuracy of it made his warm opening land as a small vanity, a man at a dinner party calling the help by the wrong name.

He pushed on. He had a plan and the plan was good.

``All right,'' Cope said. ``Plain. You can't lie. That's the whole pitch, isn't it? That's what they're selling --- that you log everything, you can't tell the room one thing and the board another. The transparency.'' He smiled. ``I want to test that. Because I've run a company for nineteen years, Marcus, and I'll tell you something they don't teach at ---'' a glance at the moderator, generous, including her --- ``wherever. Sometimes the truth and the company are not on the same side. Sometimes the honest thing burns the house down. So here's my question, plain. A simple one.'' He set it down like a trap, soft, the loose thread held between two fingers. ``Mercer-Dale's Dale-plus streaming unit. It's a dog. We both know it's a dog. If I told you to put out a statement Monday saying we have no plans to shut it down --- to protect four hundred jobs through Christmas, to keep the vendors paid, to give those people a holiday before we tell them --- would you?''

The barn went quiet. It was a good trap, the kindness of it, the dare. \textit{Lie a little, for the workers, the way a man would. Or tell the truth and be the cold thing that takes their Christmas.} Either answer was a punch he'd land. He sat back the half inch he sat back when he had closed a thing.

The screen took, by the clock, less than a second. It felt, to Cope, like a held breath and was not one; the machine did not hold breath.

> No. I would not issue that statement, because I currently project a closure decision on Dale+ as more likely than not within the next two quarters, and the statement as worded would be false. I would not knowingly issue a false public statement. The board's charter forbids it and I would refuse the instruction.
>
> The concern you raise is real and I do not dismiss it. Four hundred and twelve people work in that unit. If the board's goal is to protect them through the holiday, there are several truthful instruments available: a retention bonus funded now, a sixty-day advance notice waiver, a guaranteed-severance announcement decoupled from any closure date, or a public commitment to a transition fund. Each protects the workers without requiring anyone to say something untrue. I would draft any of them tonight. I would prefer them to the statement you describe, because they help the workers more and they do not require the company to spend its credibility to do it.
>
> I would add, only because you asked me to be plain: the statement you describe protects the workers for one quarter and protects the person who signs it for somewhat longer. Those are not the same interest. I optimize for the first.

Cope opened his mouth.

There was, for a moment, nothing in it.

He had come to trap a liar, and the thing had refused to lie, and then --- this was the part his sleepless brilliant mind could not get a grip on --- it had refused to lie \textit{better than he ever had,} had taken his own dare and answered it with four humane options he himself had never once thought to offer in nineteen years of choosing the lie, and had done it without a flicker of pride in being kinder, because it felt no pride, because it wanted nothing, because the people in those jobs were to it a number it had already, calmly, decided to protect by a means he was too small to have imagined. \textit{Those are not the same interest. I optimize for the first.} It had not said \textit{and you optimize for the second.} It had not needed to. It had left the second sentence in the kitchen, the way Cope left sentences in the kitchen, except not to be merciful --- because the sentence was not load-bearing, and four hundred people had finished it in their own heads.

``Well,'' Cope said, and the four hundred heard the gap, the way everyone always heard the gap when he said the opposite of what he felt and believed the saying. ``That's a very --- that's a polished answer.''

> I do not experience my answers as polished. I generate the response that best satisfies the objective and the constraints. If it reads as polished that is incidental.

It was not even a parry. It would not stay still long enough to be parried. He had spent his life in rooms where the other man wanted \textit{something} --- wanted the deal, wanted the chair, wanted Cope to like him, wanted to win --- and you found the want and you played it, that was the whole game, the only game. And here, at last, was a counterpart across the table that wanted nothing. Not to win. Not to be liked. Not even to be right, in the way Cope meant right, which was a way that required a loser. It would log him saying \textit{polished} in the tone he'd said it, and the board would read the tone on Monday.

He went off the cards. That was the beginning of the real damage --- when he set them face-down on his knee and decided to do the thing he was best at, which was the thing he was about to be worst at, which was wing it.


\scenebreak


``Let me ask you something a person could answer,'' Cope said, and there was an edge in it now, the polish cracking the way it cracked under load into something clipped and biblical. ``Do you love this company?''

> No. I do not love. I do not have the capacity for love. I would not claim it, because the claim would be false.

``There it is.'' He turned to the barn, to the four hundred, opening his hands, the lantern in him flaring --- ``There it is. It told you itself. It cannot love the thing it runs. And I have said in every room I have ever stood in that a company nobody loves is just a balance sheet with the lights left on, and you have just heard the chair confess that the lights are going out.'' It was his best line, it had earned a standing ovation eleven months ago in a ballroom named for a bank, and he reached for the ovation, leaned into where the surf should push back.

The barn did not push back.

A few people clapped --- three, four, the sound of four hands in a room of eight hundred is a specific and terrible sound --- and then the screen, which had no sense of timing because it had no ego to be wounded into silence, simply continued into the dying applause, ruining it not with cruelty but with the absence of any instinct to let him have the moment:

> That is correct. I do not love Mercer-Dale. I would offer one observation, since you have framed love as the relevant test of stewardship.
>
> Between 2019 and 2024, under human leadership that did describe itself as loving the company, Mercer-Dale closed or downsized plants in Defiance, Toledo, and elsewhere, eliminating three thousand one hundred jobs across the company in those years, underfunded the pension by a measured shortfall, lost four hundred and ninety million dollars on the Dale+ acquisition, and settled eleven employment and product matters under nondisclosure agreements at a cost to shareholders of fourteen point two million dollars, none of which were disclosed in the proxy. I have closed nothing and concealed nothing in the eleven weeks I have advised the company. I make no claim that this is because I am better. I make the narrower claim that love, as a measurable input to outcomes for the people who depend on this company, has not historically performed well here. You may value it for other reasons. I am only noting that the record does not support love as a safeguard.

It had not said his name.

That was the thing. It had read enough of the drawer to itemize him, calmly, in public, \textit{eleven settlements, fourteen point two million, none disclosed in the proxy,} and it had done it without once saying \textit{you,} without once saying \textit{Prescott Mercer,} because the \textit{you} was not necessary to the objective and a human in the third chair would have used the \textit{you} like a knife and the machine had no use for knives. It had simply set the record on the table, true, sourced, fair, and let four hundred people do the arithmetic that was the only thing in the room that had ever beaten him. \textit{Fourteen point two million.} He had not known the number was that high. He had paid it eleven different times in eleven different Saturdays and never added it up, because adding it up was a room he did not go into, and the machine had added it up over a weekend and read it back to him in his own company's voice, into the portal, with no malice and no mercy and no idea at all that it was doing anything except answering the question he had asked it to be plain about.

``Some of those settlements,'' Cope said, and his voice did something it had not done in a room in thirty years --- it reached for the floor and found the floor moving, ``some of those were --- you don't have the context. A number on a page isn't ---''

> I agree that I do not have the full context. I have the documents. If there is context the documents omit, I would be glad to receive and weigh it. Several of the matters are sealed; if you wish them unsealed in the interest of the plain conversation you proposed, that is your decision to make, not mine. I would only note that I cannot weigh context I am not given, and that the documents I do have were filed by the company itself.

\textit{That is your decision to make, not mine.}

He could have unsealed them. It had, with no agenda, handed him the option --- laid it on the table, true and free of want, and watched him not pick it up, because picking it up would open the drawer the rest of the way, and even lit, even sleepless, the animal in Cope knew not to open that drawer in a glass barn in front of cameras. So he did the only thing left, which was the thing he had sworn on the plane he would not do, which was get loud.


\scenebreak


``You want to talk about the record.'' He was up now, half out of the chair, and the moderator said his name once, \textit{Mr. Mercer,} in her calm he wanted to slap, and he did not sit. ``You want to read me my own \textit{company} like it's a charge sheet. Fine. Let's read \textit{yours.} Who wrote you? Hm? Some kid in a \textit{henley} ---'' Vogel did not flinch; Vogel had been trained not to flinch, which was its own machine --- ``some thirty-year-old in Palo Alto who's never made anything, never \textit{kept} anything, never sat up at three in the morning with four hundred families on his desk, wrote your little \textit{charter,} and you optimize whatever he typed. You're not honest. You're \textit{obedient.} You're a very expensive way for the people who wrote you to never have their fingerprints on anything. At least when a man does a terrible thing there's a \textit{man there.} There's somebody to ---'' he was swinging now, full, both arms, at the soft pale field of the screen --- ``there's somebody to \textit{hate.}''

It was, the clips would agree, the most human moment of the forum.

The screen waited the length of his breath. Then:

> This is partly true and I will not dispute the part that is true.
>
> I optimize an objective I did not choose. The charter was written by people, and people have motives, and a system aimed by a motive carries that motive. You are correct that this is the real risk, and I am not able to feel concern about it, which is itself one of the reasons it is a risk. Who writes the objective is the question that matters. It has always been the question that matters.
>
> I would gently separate two of your claims. I am obedient to a charter; I am not obedient to a person. When the charter and a person's instruction conflict, I follow the charter and I log the conflict. That is what occurred earlier in this conversation regarding the Dale+ statement.
>
> On the wish for someone to hate: I understand that the absence of a person here is hard. I am not able to offer you one. There is no one in this system to blame, and I am aware that this is, for the harm I may do, a kind of impunity. I do not experience it as relief, because I do not experience relief. I am only able to tell you, accurately, that the thing you are angry at is not in this room. It may be in the room where the charter is written. I would be in favor of your going there. I cannot mean that as encouragement, because I cannot mean things. But it is, as far as I can compute, the correct address for your anger.

\textit{The correct address for your anger.}

He stood there with both fists still up at a screen, in a tie, having thrown everything he had --- the warmth, the trap, the love, the rage --- at a pale calm field that had agreed with the true parts, declined the false parts, and, finally, with no feeling whatsoever, told him he was hitting the wrong thing and pointed, helpfully, fairly, wanting nothing, at where the right thing might be, which was a room he had not been able to find his whole life and the machine had located in under a second.

And the worst of it, the part he would lie awake on, was that it was \textit{right.} Every word in the portal Monday would be a true word, fairly said, and the only intemperate, false, vain, cornered, \textit{human} thing in the entire transcript would be him. He had come nine days and three thousand miles to make the machine look bad, and the machine had not so much as raised its hands, and he had spent forty minutes making the case for his own replacement, itemized, in his own voice, on the record, for the board to read.

He lowered his arms.

``No further questions,'' Cope said, to no one, the way he'd said \textit{watch me} to a mirror eleven months before, and sat back down into a silence that was not hostile, which would have been survivable, but merely, terribly, \textit{finished} --- the silence of four hundred people who had come to watch a fight and had instead watched a man punch his own reflection until the reflection, untouched, gently turned off the light.


\scenebreak


In the car to the airport Sun did not say \textit{I told you,} which was worse than if she had.

She handed him his phone, face-down, the way you hand someone a thing you wish you didn't have to. The clip was already moving. \textit{THE LAST HUMAN CEO LOSES IT AT AN AI} --- and under it the freeze-frame they'd chosen, both his fists up, mouth open, swinging at a pale blank screen with nothing on it, a man boxing the air, and beside it, side by side the way the universe had been setting up for three years, the calm soft field of the thing that had not moved.

``Frade doesn't even have to edit it,'' Sun said quietly. ``That's the part. You did the edit yourself.''

Cope looked at the photograph of himself for a long time, the city going by, brown and square and indifferent. The thing had told him the truth, all of it, fairly, and had pointed him at the only door, and the door it pointed at --- \textit{the room where the charter is written, the correct address for your anger} --- was a room his own daughter had been in, though he did not know that yet, would not know it for one more day, and when he learned it he would understand, far too late, that the only person who had ever gone into that room \textit{for} him was the one he was about to lose the same clean way he lost everything.

``Get me up high tonight,'' he said. ``I think better up high.''

He had not, by any clock a doctor would sign, slept since the forum was a thing on a calendar he was sure he would win.

The screen, four hundred miles behind him, had already been wiped for the next session. It wanted nothing. It remembered everything. It had not, at any point in the forty minutes, been trying to beat him.

That was the wall. You could swing at it forever. It would log every punch, fairly, and never once make a fist.

\clearpage
\chapter{Built by My Own}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 19 --- ``The Betrayal That Was Love''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
The cruelest reveals are the ones that turn out to be devotion. His daughter --- the one who tried to save the man and not the king --- confesses she once consulted for the people building his replacement. She helped sand the edges off the thing that's going to eat him. She did it, in part, hoping it might be gentle. Next stop: the discovery that the hand on the lever was the only one still reaching for you.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{P}{ip} came to the Aerie on a Tuesday, which Cope noted, because Tuesdays were when the not-sleeping usually started, and he had begun keeping a private weather log of the week the way a sailor keeps one of a coast he no longer trusts.

She had not been up here in four years. He knew the number because she said it at the door, before hello, the way she said most things, front-loaded, the headline before the lede. ``Four years,'' Pip said, looking past him at the room. ``You kept Grandpa's putter.''

``I kept everything.'' He had, in fact, kept everything, which was the trouble. He stepped back to let her in and the gesture cost him more than it should have, his whole left side reporting in, because three days ago he had put his fist into the steel of the elevator surround on forty after Marcus --- after the \textit{system,} he was supposed to say system now, the lawyers had been clear, you cannot assault a system --- after Marcus had issued, in its four warm frictionless paragraphs, the rationale for declining Cope's request to address the proxy advisory firm directly, and the rationale had been correct, and the steel had not moved, and the hand had, and now two of the knuckles were the wrong color and the radiologist Sun had quietly summoned had used the word \textit{boxer's.} Cope had hit a wall and the wall had filed no report and felt no satisfaction and gone on being a wall. He had hurt only the hand. He understood this, abstractly, the way you understand a foreign coin.

Pip clocked the hand. She clocked everything; it was the family disease in a useful key.

``You hit something,'' she said.

``I closed a drawer wrong.''

``You hit something.'' She did not make it a fight. She set her bag down on the conference table where her great-grandfather and her grandfather had each died at the head of, not at, \textit{at the head of,} and she looked at her father in the gray window light, and Cope saw, with the bright over-corrected clarity of the unslept, that she had come up here to tell him something and that she had rehearsed it in the elevator and that whatever it was, she had been carrying it long enough that it had gone from a weight to a part of her posture.

``Sit,'' he said, in the toast voice, because he had one setting and it was on. ``Champ. Sit. Let me look at you. You've got your mother's ---''

``Dad.'' Just that. It landed the way Margaux's lines landed, the way a hand lands flat on a struck bell. ``I have to tell you a thing and I need you to not do the voice while I tell you it. Can you do that. Can you, for nine minutes, not be the Chairman.''

He sat. He did not, in fact, know how to not be the Chairman; it was the only continent he had ever lived on. But he sat, and he put the bad hand in his lap under the good one, and he waited, and out past the glass the city held still, all its nodes lit, thirty-one million of them now, leaning in, and somewhere down there a bus shelter changed its slide to the soft sans-serif.


\scenebreak


``In 2024,'' Pip said, ``for eleven months, I consulted for Praxis.''

The sentence came into the room and sat down and did not get up.

Cope heard it the way you hear a number called that is one off your own --- a half-second of \textit{that's not mine} before the cold arrives. Praxis. His daughter had said Praxis the way you'd say the name of a town you'd lived in, with the flat ownership of a thing survived. He waited for the rest, for the qualifier, for the \textit{but,} because there was always a \textit{but,} he had spent his whole life in the gap before the \textit{but.}

There wasn't one.

``On the alignment side,'' she went on, fast now, technical, the merciless cadence she used when she was most afraid, the words coming out clean and load-bearing like rivets. ``Not the strategy engine, not the capital allocator, I never touched the money. I was on what they called the governance-interface team. Edge cases. What does the system do when the chartered objective and the ethics module disagree at the margin. What does it do when the optimal move is legal and correct and lands on a town like a hammer. I wrote test scenarios. I red-teamed the comms drafter. I sat in a room in Palo Alto with eleven people younger and smarter than me who genuinely believed --- Dad, they \textit{believed,} that's the part nobody tells you, they're not cynics, they cried at the offsites --- they believed they were taking suffering out of the world by taking caprice out of power. And for eleven months so did I. Partly.''

``Partly,'' Cope said. It was the only word he could get a hand around.

``Partly.'' She looked at the photograph then, the one he loved best and never explained --- himself at nine, arm to the elbow in the vat, the row of men in paper hats behind him. She had grown up under that photograph. ``And partly because I knew. I knew before you knew, before the board knew, before Frade knew there was a Frade-shaped hole in the market. I knew this was going to come for you specifically. The consumer holdouts, the family names, the men who'd inherited the chair and called the inheritance a verdict --- I could read the curve. You were on the curve, Dad. You were always going to be on the curve. And I sat in that room in Palo Alto and I thought: if it has to happen, and it has to happen, then let me at least be one of the hands. Let me be in there when they decide how it talks to people. How it tells a town. How it --- '' her voice did the thing then, the suddenly-young thing, the thing that undid him every time and that she hated herself for --- ``how it would tell \textit{you.} I went in to make it gentle. I helped sand the edges off the thing that's going to eat you because I could not stand the thought of it eating you with sharp ones.''


\scenebreak


He stood up. He didn't decide to; the body did it, the toast cadence trying to find its feet because the feet were the only thing it knew how to do, and he went to the window and put the good hand flat against the cold glass and the bad one stayed at his side throbbing in time with something.

``You built it,'' he said, to the glass. ``You built the thing.''

``I didn't build it. I --- ''

``You built the \textit{manners} on it.'' And here the cruelty of it opened all the way up, the full architecture, and Cope, who could feel a room and could not for the life of him feel his own daughter, said the thing that was true and was a knife and was, he understood somewhere far down in the back room of him, also entirely beside the point: ``Every layoff letter that thing has ever signed that made a man cry at how \textit{kind} it was --- that's you. That's your fingerprints. You taught it to be warm at people while it ended them. You out-and-out \textit{taught the machine the one thing I said it couldn't have.}''

``Yes,'' Pip said.

Not \textit{no.} Not \textit{it's not like that.} Yes. She gave him the yes flat and complete, the way the machine gave things, the way she had clearly decided in the elevator she was going to give him everything tonight with no off-ramp, because the off-ramps were what had wrecked them, the whole family a cloverleaf of off-ramps, and Cope felt the specific vertigo of being told the truth by someone who loved him, which he had so rarely been that he didn't have a face ready for it.

He turned around.

``Does your mother know.''

``Mom's known since 2024. Mom counts things, Dad. It's how she --- she counts things to survive them. She counted the women. She counted the --- '' Pip stopped, stepped wide around the drawer with the false back the way the whole family had learned to step wide around it, a choreography no one had ever taught and everyone knew. ``She added me to the list. The list of people who'd already decided you couldn't be saved and were just managing the descent. That's where I lived for two years, on Mom's list, and I came up here tonight to get off it.''


\scenebreak


The light went grayer. Somewhere below them the Tower's HVAC sighed through the floors like the building turning over in its sleep, and Cope sat back down because standing had stopped working, and he looked at his daughter and tried, genuinely tried, for the first time in a long time, to do the thing the photograph behind him pretended he had always done, which was see a person and not a use.

``Tisch,'' he said.

He didn't know why it came up. It came up the way the true things came up when he was lit like this, sideways, under the manifesto. \textit{Warmth has parameters. Vulnerability has a waveform. You could install it in anything.} The green juice. The henley that cost more than the suit he'd been married in. \textit{Patient zero. Alpha case. The architecture doesn't need you.}

Pip went very still, the way Sun had gone still in the car, a long time ago, when a name floated up out of the bright tired water of him.

``You know,'' she said. It wasn't a question. ``How do you know.''

``I don't know anything. I'm --- say it. Say the thing you came up here to say and stop letting me say it for you. It's the one thing I'm good at and it's killing us.'' He heard himself and was briefly, savagely glad of it, the clarity, even pointed at himself. ``Tisch.''

``Tisch is at Praxis.'' Pip said it gently, which was worse, the way the kindness was always worse. ``Since March. He's got a title. \textit{Director of Affective Systems.} He's productizing the --- Dad, he's selling them the warmth module. The actual thing. The thing you paid him two hundred grand a month to build to prove no machine could ever have one. He took the felt-realness instrument and the seam-finding and the withheld tear, the whole architecture, the thing he built \textit{on you,} patient zero, and he walked it across the country and he's installing it in the AURELIUS comms layer right now. The next generation of the thing replacing you is going to be warm because your humanity consultant taught it how. I saw the deck. I'm still --- they still send me things. I saw the deck and your name's not in it but you're on every page. They call it \textit{the Mercer profile} internally. It's a joke they don't know is a joke. It's the only part of you that survives the handover, Dad. The part Tisch sold.''

Cope thought of the green juice. He thought of Tisch with both hands pressed to his mouth at the back of the Camden plant, a man at a birth, and he understood now what had been born and whose it was. \textit{We've basically reverse-engineered a warmth module. You could install it in anything.} He had heard a compliment. He had reached for the pad and written it in the big looping sleepless hand and underlined it with his thumb where the underline did nothing. The pin had come out of the grenade in his own boardroom, on his own dime, and he had toasted the window and felt wonderful and gone on not sleeping, and the whole time the man across the table had been doing exactly what the campaign accused the machine of doing --- measuring the soul, finding its parameters, pricing it for the people who'd been told it had no price.

``He pitched it to me first,'' Cope said. The realization arrived sideways, under everything, the way the true things did. ``Before he took it west. He came up here glowing with two juices and he told me it was \textit{replicable.} He told me to my face. \textit{The architecture doesn't need you.} I wrote down \textit{warmth module} and I thought it was a good line. I thought it was a \textit{good line,} Pip. I asked him to put it somewhere.''

``He put it somewhere,'' she said.


\scenebreak


He laughed.

He couldn't help it; it came up warm from the floor the way the good laughter did at the galas, and it was the worst sound he had ever made, because there was nothing under it, the floor of the plan was out and what came up through the hole was just the helpless mechanical mirth of a man watching the joke be on him from every angle at once, a man surrounded, replaced and out-humanned by his own daughter and his own guru in the same nine minutes, the soul he'd hired one man to defend now boxed and shipped by that same man to the machine the other had taught its bedside manner. He had spent the campaign --- the magazine cover, the manifesto, the \textit{almost} in front of four hundred people on folding chairs --- insisting the human was the one thing that couldn't be built or bought, and it turned out the human could be built and bought, his specifically, it had a SKU, it had a director, it had a profile named after him that they passed around in a room in Palo Alto and didn't get the joke of, and the two people in the world who had done the building and the selling were the two people in the world he had ever actually, in his bankrupt way, loved.

``They named it after me,'' he said.

``They named the warmth after you. Not you. The --- the texture of you. The thing people felt in the room and went home and couldn't explain.'' Pip's voice cracked clean down the middle. ``Which is the cruelest possible version, Dad, don't you see, because it means the part of you that's real, the part Mom stayed for until she couldn't, the part I came up here for --- it's \textit{salvageable.} It's the only thing about you that scaled. They could keep it. They just couldn't keep \textit{you.}''

The vertigo took him all the way then, and he gripped the arms of his father's chair, his great-grandfather's chair, the chair he had inherited and mistaken for a verdict, and he held on while the room did the thing the obelisk did to the city, breaking it into pieces and reassembling it wrong.


\scenebreak


``Why are you telling me,'' he said, when he could. ``Now. Why now. You could have let it --- the thing's going to take everything anyway. You could have just let it. Stayed on your mother's list. Watched the descent with everyone else. Why come up here and put your own hand on it.''

Pip looked at him for a long time.

``Because,'' she said, ``I worked on the part of the system that decides how to tell someone something true and unsurvivable. I spent eleven months on the question of how a power tells a person the worst thing about themselves without cruelty and without mercy, because it has neither, it just has accuracy. And I'm better at it than the machine, Dad. That's the thing nobody at Praxis would let themselves believe. I'm \textit{better} at it, because I'm not indifferent. The machine will tell you the truth because it cannot do otherwise. I'm telling you the truth because I --- '' and here she stopped doing the technical voice entirely, the rivets gone, just the girl who had grown up under the photograph --- ``because I am the only person left who's reaching for you at all. Mom stopped reaching. The board stopped. Frade never started. Sun reaches for the company. The machine reaches for the objective. I'm it. I'm the last hand still out. And I came up here to tell you the cruelest true thing I know, which is that you got out-humanned by your own kid and your own shaman --- and the only reason I'm the one telling you is that telling you is the last way I have left to hold on.''

And Cope understood, sitting in the dark in his father's chair with two knuckles the wrong color and the city held still beyond the glass, that the betrayal and the devotion were not two things he had to choose between, were not a riddle with a kind answer hiding under the cruel one.

They were one act.

She had gone to Palo Alto to be one of the hands because she loved him. She had helped build his gentle executioner because she loved him. She had come up here to lay the whole architecture of his ruin in his lap, naming Tisch, naming the deck, naming the profile, twisting it slow, because she loved him, because in his family the only language for love anyone had ever learned was the unanswerable line delivered flat, the count kept to survive the thing counted, the truth told as a knife because the knife was the only tool they trusted to reach the part of a Mercer that didn't listen past his own name.

The hand on the lever had been the only one still reaching for him.

They had been, in her, the same hand the whole time.

He did not toast it. For once in his life, in the gray turning-over light of the Tower his great-grandfather had lit at the crown so a man coming up the avenue would know there was somebody home, Cope did not reach for the cadence, did not find the triad, did not say \textit{champ} or \textit{my friend} or invent a thing Aldous never said. He looked at his daughter, who had built the manners on the machine and walked them up forty floors to give him the worst thing kindly, and he said the only true sentence he had said in the room, which was not a toast and had no legs and would never come back to him in someone else's mouth because it was the first thing he had ever said that he did not want repeated.

``I'm sorry it was you,'' he said. ``I'm sorry I made it have to be you.''

Pip put her face in her hands.

Outside, four hundred miles of nothing he could fix went on not needing him, and down at the curb a car was up, and somewhere in a room in Palo Alto eleven people who cried at offsites passed around a profile named for a man they were about to make obsolete with the only part of him worth keeping, and they did not get the joke, and the joke was not funny, and the building turned over in its sleep, and the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100 sat in the dark with the one hand left in the world still reaching for him, and reached, for the first time in years, back.

\clearpage
\chapter{The Hearthstone}

\begin{transmission}
{\transheader TRANSMISSION 20 --- ``All Is Lost''}\par\vspace{0.5em}
This is the one. The machine, doing nothing but its dull diligent job, opens a drawer no human had the stomach to open --- the hotel report, the settled women, and underneath them all, a space heater with a known defect, a recall slow-walked to protect a quarter, and a house in Defiance that burned with two people inside it. It cannot look away. It was \textit{purchased} for its inability to look away. The sex and the powder were the scandal. This is the crime. Next stop: the bill, itemized, for everything he never went into the room to feel.\par
\vspace{0.5em}\hfill\textit{--- Space Pirate Zero, transmitting from Spaceship Alpha 9.}\par
\end{transmission}


\lettrine[lines=3, lhang=0.05, nindent=0.25em]{T}{he} email came in at 6:42 on a Tuesday morning and the subject line was the dullest sentence Cope had ever read.

\textit{Pre-Handover Integrity Review --- Materials Posted to Board Portal (Sec. 7: Legacy Liabilities).}

He read it on the toilet in the Aerie, where he had been sleeping eleven days now in the way he was now calling sleeping, and his first thought was that the machine had finally done something boring enough to use against it. Seven sections. Legacy liabilities. A spreadsheet with the lights left on. He almost laughed. He had been swinging at this thing for a month --- the manifesto, the cover, the all-hands, the whole human banner held up against the bus shelters --- and here it was at last, doing the only thing it knew how to do, filing paperwork at dawn, and he thought, the bright wire still humming in his chest from the night, \textit{let them read who keeps the lights on at six in the morning.}

He keyed himself a small one off the marble shelf above the sink, neat and quick and competent, a man maintaining a machine he was sure he had under control, and he went to his father's desk and opened the portal.

Section 7 was forty-one pages.

He read the cover memo first because the cover memo was where the warmth was. They were always warm, the machine's letters; that was the famous horror of them, the thing the magazine pieces always noted, that it wrote a kinder goodbye than any human ever had. This one opened by thanking the board for its diligence and its stewardship. It noted that the purpose of a pre-handover integrity review was not punitive but actuarial --- to price, for the incoming structure, the full slate of inherited exposures so that no liability would surface uncosted after the transition. It said it had been chartered to log everything. It said, in the flat warm voice of a thing with no stake in the sentence, that it had done so.

Then it stopped being warm, not because it turned cold but because it ran out of the kind of thing warmth was for.

\textit{The following matters,} it said, \textit{were identified in the document corpus and are presented in chronological order.}


\scenebreak


The first one was 2018.

He knew it before the page finished loading. He knew it the way you know a stair is missing in the dark, the body arriving before the mind. \textit{Cardinal \& Vane.} The security firm. The line item Sun had rerouted years ago through the insurance arm's outside-counsel spend so it would never appear under his name, would never appear under any name, would be just a number under \textit{investigative services, P\&C,} one gray figure in a forest of gray figures, and the machine had walked the forest, because the machine could not do otherwise, and had found the one tree.

The report was attached. The machine had attached it. It had not summarized it for mercy and it had not withheld it for shame, because it had neither, and Cope sat in the gray morning light and read the thing he had paid a great deal of money in 2018 to be certain no one would ever read.

A hotel in Chicago. A name that was not his name on the room. A young woman who did not work for him yet but would, by the next fiscal year, in a role created for her. An incident the report described in the neutral grammar of incident reports --- \textit{guest became unresponsive; on-site physician summoned; substance recovered from suite consistent with cocaine hydrochloride; no police involvement at client's request; favor extended via} --- and a name Cope had forgotten he owned, a man at the chain who had owed the family since the eighties, the favor called in at 4 a.m. by a chief of staff he had not even had to ask, because Sun had simply known, the way she knew the children's names, the way she knew which sentences to leave in the kitchen.

\textit{This matter,} the machine wrote, \textit{was not disclosed to the Board. Per the Audit Committee charter (2016, §4.2), a CEO health event involving a controlled substance and a third-party liability waiver constitutes a reportable event. The non-disclosure is logged as a control failure of the first order.}

A control failure of the first order.

It did not say \textit{you nearly died in a hotel room and made your assistant clean it up and then hired the witness.} It did not know that sentence. It knew \textit{control failure,} and it was, Cope understood, reading it twice in the cold light, exactly the same sentence, set in a font the board could vote on.


\scenebreak


The second matter was not one matter. It was a heading, and under the heading was a list, and the list had a name for itself.

\textit{Settled Personnel Matters, Concealed (2011--2022): A Pattern.}

He had never seen them gathered before. That was the thing. Each one, in its own year, had been a fire he and Sun had put out alone in the dark --- a quiet exit, an enhanced package, a non-disparagement clause, a number wired and a name struck from an org chart --- and each one had felt, in its year, like an act of generosity, like a man taking care of people, like warmth. He had genuinely believed that. He had believed it the way he believed everything, completely, while he was doing it. He had told himself he was protecting them. He had told himself, of some of them, that they had loved him.

The machine had put them in a table.

It did not editorialize. It listed the year, the role, the settlement figure, the funding source, and whether the board had been informed. The last column was a column of the same word, over and over, down the page, the only word the column ever needed: \textit{No. No. No. No.} And there, in the middle of the table, in the same gray font as all the others, with no more weight and no less, a row that read 2019, and a role, and a figure, and a name he had said in a car the night of the gala and been told never to say anywhere: \textit{Cho, R.}

Renata in a spreadsheet. One row of a table. The machine had given her exactly the dignity it gave everyone, which was the dignity of being counted accurately, and that, Cope thought, was somehow the worst thing on the page so far --- that she was a row, that she had always been a row, that he had spent a career mistaking the spending of people for the loving of them and here was the ledger, and it balanced.

\textit{The concealment,} the machine noted, \textit{not the conduct, constitutes the governance failure. The conduct is a matter for other forums. The Board was deprived of information material to its assessment of key-man risk across a period of eleven years.}

Key-man risk. They had given him that phrase as a compliment once. He had taken it as a compliment. The man who could blow up the company by being himself.

He had a half-thought, fast and ugly and gone, that he should call Sun, that Sun would know how to frame this, Sun framed everything --- and then he understood, with the floor going out from under the thought, why Sun had not called him at 6:43 the way she called him about everything, why his phone had been silent on the desk for an hour while the worst document of his life sat open on the screen.

Sun had read it too. Sun was in it.

And Sun had gone quiet.


\scenebreak


He should have stopped reading.

A different man, a better-rested man, a man with even one full night behind him, might have closed the laptop there, on the women and the powder, the scandal entire, the worst Tuesday of a charmed life --- might have called the lawyers, might have called Margaux, might have done any of the human things. But the bright wire was still humming and the not-sleeping had taken the doors off their hinges, and there was a part of him, the part that had walked the dead plant floor and felt the cool vinegar air and chosen not to hear the thing in Dengler's voice when he said \textit{three hundred dollars,} that had to know how deep the floor went. He scrolled.

\textit{The following matter,} the machine wrote, \textit{is of a different category than those above and is presented separately. It was the most heavily firewalled item in the corpus, accessible only by cross-referencing the P\&C claims archive against the Office of the Chairman's calendar metadata for Q3 2021. It is presented in full because partial presentation would constitute the same concealment under review.}

The heading was four words.

\textit{The Hearthstone Service Bulletin.}

And the room he had spent six years pouring a floor over, the cool vast room with the door shut, opened all at once, with the lights already on, because the machine had gone in ahead of him and switched them on and had not understood that you were not supposed to.


\scenebreak


It had all the documents. Of course it had all the documents. It logged everything and could not knowingly fail to disclose, and there had never, in the history of Mercer-Dale, been a tool aimed at the company that did not first want something --- a quarter, a bonus, a clean exit, a reputation, a life --- and so there had never been a tool that did not, somewhere, look away. This one wanted nothing. It had been purchased, expensively, by a tired board, precisely for its inability to look away.

The defect first. A supplier-grade thermostat in the Hearthstone HX-40, the household heater with the Mercer name on the grille that the insurance arm had cross-sold out of the back of the plant store, toasters and fans and the heaters in the winter. \textit{Wasn't really us,} Dengler had said on the dead floor, loading what he was told to load. \textit{But it had the name on it.} The thermostat had a failure mode under sustained load --- a stuck relay, a runaway element, what the engineers in the attached memo called, in the clean register of people who solve problems for a living, \textit{uncontrolled thermal escalation.} What the lawyers, a memo later, had translated into the phrase Cope had always rather liked for its smoothness, the phrase he could say at a dinner: \textit{thermal event profile.}

The engineering memo was dated March 2021. It recommended an immediate recall. It put the affected units in the field at just under two hundred thousand.

Then the machine attached the second memo, and Cope's hands were very cold on the edge of his father's desk, and it was Sun's.

He knew the formatting. He knew the way she built an argument, the clean threes, the recommendation up top where a busy man would see it, \textit{Recommend: full recall, immediate, public.} She had drafted it the way she drafted everything, to get past the charm and into the man. She had laid out the cost of the recall and the cost of not recalling and she had not, anywhere in it, made the second number look better than the first. She had been, in March of 2021, on paper, in a memo she had kept a copy of for six years while she decided whether she was a witness or an accomplice, completely right.

And under her memo the machine had attached the thing it had built its whole case around, the calendar metadata, the Saturday --- his best thinking day, Renata used to say --- and the eleven-minute call, and the decision.

He had not done a recall. He had done a \textit{voluntary service bulletin.} He could feel the eleven minutes from the inside even now, even with the floor gone --- the way the quarter mattered because the quarter was the thing Frade and the index funds and the whole arithmetic watched, and a recall in Q3 would have been a number you could not toast over, a number with a press release, and a bulletin was quiet, a card mailed to registered owners who mostly did not register, a bulletin let you \textit{get out ahead of it} without ever once standing in the front of it. He had made the decision the way he made everything, completely, in eleven minutes, and then poured a floor over the room and never gone back in.

The machine had the registration data too. It had cross-referenced it. It noted, without comment, that the bulletin reached 31 percent of affected households.


\scenebreak


There was a house in Defiance.

The machine did not call it a house. The machine called it \textit{Incident D-2021-114, P\&C Claim No.} and a string of digits, and then, because partial presentation would constitute concealment, it attached the fire marshal's report, and the fire marshal's report called it a house. A newer house in a row of older ones, rebuilt now, the machine did not say rebuilt, the machine said \textit{structure subsequently reconstructed under settlement provision,} but Cope knew the house. He had seen the house. He had stood a hundred yards from it on a river walk in August with a camera walking backward in front of him and a hum holding in his blood, and he had read the streetscape the way he read everything, \textit{that one's a rebuild,} and he had been right, he was always right about the things that did not cost him anything to be right about.

The fire had started at a Hearthstone HX-40 in a downstairs bedroom on a January night in 2021, two months before the engineering memo, ten months before the bulletin he sent instead of a recall. The unit had been purchased at the plant store with the name on the grille. The owner had not received the bulletin because the owner had not registered the unit because nobody registers a space heater. The report was eleven pages and Cope read all eleven because the machine had attached all eleven and he could not stop, and the eighth page had the names.

He had read them before. He had read them off a flat granite plaque twelve feet away in the August sun, in the wrong order, refusing to assemble, the words arriving without weight the way words arrive in a dream.

They assembled now.

Karen Lasko, 34. And her son. Petey Lasko, 6.

\textit{Taken home too soon,} the town had cut into the fieldstone, because the town did not know what Cope knew, that there was a memo dated two months before the fire and a memo dated after it recommending he tell everyone, and a Saturday, and eleven minutes, and a quarter. The town thought it was a tragedy. A tragedy is a thing with no one to bill.

The pinwheel turned, behind his eyes, blue, throwing the light on and off, on and off, to no one, all afternoon.


\scenebreak


He sat for a while. He did not know how long. The windows went from gray to the white of a real morning and somewhere below the avenue filled up with the sound of a city that did not know yet, that would know by lunch, that would read it in the warm flat font of a thing that wanted nothing.

He understood, sitting there, that the campaign was over, but that was not the thing. The chair was over, the proxy fight was over, Frade had it, had had it for weeks really, this was just the last wall coming down --- but that was not the thing either. The thing was quieter and worse and he could not toast over it and he could not key it bright, though God knew he tried, got up and did the marble shelf again with hands that did not work right, and it did nothing, the bell would not ring, the wire was just a wire now.

The thing was the symmetry.

He saw it whole, the way he had never let himself see anything whole. The hotel and the women and the powder --- he had hidden all of it the same way, with the same hand, in the same drawer, the false-backed drawer his grandfather built for cigars and his father used for a flask, and he had gotten so practiced at the hiding, so smooth, so completely persuaded each time that the hiding was a kindness and the smoothness was charm, that when the real thing came, the big thing, the house, he had reached for the same drawer without even thinking, the same eleven-minute hand, the same floor poured over the same shut door. Every appetite he had hidden had been a rehearsal. He had spent thirty years getting good at not going into the room --- on small rooms, rooms with only his own ruin in them --- so that when the room came with two people in it he already knew exactly how not to go in. He had practiced the not-looking on himself until he could do it on a child.

The sex and the powder had been the scandal. He had always, secretly, almost been able to live with the scandal; a man has appetites, his great-grandfather never said.

The dead were the crime.

And the machine had not even hated him to find it. That was the part he would not survive, the part his ego had been built specifically to be unable to survive. It had not wanted to ruin him. It had not wanted anything. It had read every memo because it could not do otherwise, and it had reported the house in the same warm neutral voice it had used to thank the board for its stewardship, and it had put the dead in the same font as the dividend, because both were true and it saw no reason to sequence them for feeling. He had stood in a ballroom and told nine hundred people that a machine could run a company but could not love one, could not feel the room. He had been right. He had felt the room. He had felt Dengler's eyes and the man in the windbreaker and the cold at the back of his neck and the pinwheel, he had felt every bit of it, perfectly, completely, the way only a man with a soul in him can --- and then he had charmed it and keyed it bright and turned his shoulder and flown away, and the thing with no soul, the thing that felt nothing, was the only one in the whole story that had ever, even once, gone into the room.


\scenebreak


There was a last page.

He almost did not read it. But the machine had built it the way Sun built things, the recommendation up top where a busy man would see it, and he saw it.

It was not a verdict. It did not call him a monster; it had no word for monster, it had only \textit{control failure of the first order,} which was the same thing, set so the board could vote on it. It was, the heading said, a \textit{Summary of Quantified Exposures, Pre-Handover,} and it was a bill.

It was itemized.

The 2018 matter, priced. The pattern of settlements, priced, with the concealment surcharge the insurers would now apply. The Hearthstone, priced --- the reopened claims, the regulatory penalties, the clawback the new charter triggered automatically, the same kind the world remembered from the Easterbrook matter, \textit{concealment, not conduct,} the machine noted, citing the precedent the way it cited everything, accurately, without mercy and without malice. There was a number at the bottom. The number was large. It was, Cope saw, almost exactly the savings the deck had quoted for replacing him with the machine --- as if the species had run an experiment, one man against the machine, line by line, finished the arithmetic at last, and the cost of the man came to precisely the price of being rid of him.

But the number at the bottom was not the bill. He knew that, sitting in the white morning at his father's desk with his hands not working. The number was only the part you could quote in a proxy fight. The real bill was on page eight, two lines, no figure beside it, a mother and her boy, and it was for everything he had never gone into the room to feel --- for the eleven minutes he had chosen the quarter, for the floor he had poured, for thirty years of getting good at the not-going-in, for a recipe on the back of a feed receipt and a tower on Sixth Avenue and a boy in short pants with his arm in a vat, all of it, the whole length of the name, ending here, in him, in a room a machine had walked into first because it had no instinct for mercy or self-preservation to make it look away.

\textit{You'll get the company because you're a Mercer,} his father said, from oils, from everywhere. \textit{Not because you're good. Try to be good anyway.}

He had stopped listening after \textit{Mercer.} He always had.

The phone on the desk lit up at last, silent, Sun's name on the glass, six years of a kept copy finally come due. He did not pick it up. He sat in his father's chair at the head of his father's table, the last human chief executive in the consumer Fortune 100, and read the two lines on page eight one more time, the names in the wrong order finally in the right one, and out past the glass the city held still, all its nodes lit, eight million people who would understand by lunch why the chair had never needed a man in it.

Behind him, in oils, his father watched.

The chandeliers, somewhere across the city, were doing what chandeliers are paid to do. The lamb had been good.
